    <title>A draft genome and transcriptome of common milkweed (Asclepias syriaca) as resources for evolutionary, ecological, and molecular studies in milkweeds and Apocynaceae [PeerJ]</title>

    
        <link rel="dns-prefetch" href="https://d2pdyyx74uypu5.cloudfront.net/">
    <link rel="dns-prefetch" href="http://static.peerj.com/">
<link rel="dns-prefetch" href="https://doi.org">

        
            <meta name="citation_title" content="A draft genome and transcriptome of common milkweed (Asclepias syriaca) as resources for evolutionary, ecological, and molecular studies in milkweeds and Apocynaceae"><meta name="citation_date" content="2019-09-20"><meta name="citation_doi" content="10.7717/peerj.7649"><meta name="citation_language" content="en"><meta name="citation_pdf_url" content="https://peerj.com/articles/7649.pdf"><meta name="citation_fulltext_html_url" content="https://peerj.com/articles/7649"><meta name="citation_volume" content="7"><meta name="citation_firstpage" content="e7649"><meta name="citation_keywords" content="Asclepias; Milkweed; Apocynaceae; Cardenolide; Chromosome evolution; Genome; Gentianales; Linkage mapping; Plant genome"><meta name="citation_journal_title" content="PeerJ"><meta name="citation_journal_abbrev" content="PeerJ"><meta name="citation_publisher" content="PeerJ Inc."><meta name="citation_issn" content="2167-8359"><meta name="citation_author" content="Kevin Weitemier"><meta name="citation_author_institution" content="Department of Fisheries and Wildlife, Oregon State University, Corvallis, OR, USA"><meta name="citation_author_email" content="kevin.weitemier@oregonstate.edu"><meta name="citation_author" content="Shannon C.K. Straub"><meta name="citation_author_institution" content="Department of Biology, Hobart and William Smith Colleges, Geneva, NY, USA"><meta name="citation_author" content="Mark Fishbein"><meta name="citation_author_institution" content="Department of Plant Biology, Ecology, and Evolution, Oklahoma State University, Stillwater, OK, USA"><meta name="citation_author" content="C. Donovan Bailey"><meta name="citation_author_institution" content="Department of Biology, New Mexico State University, Las Cruces, NM, USA"><meta name="citation_author" content="Richard C. Cronn"><meta name="citation_author_institution" content="Pacific Northwest Research Station, USDA Forest Service, Corvallis, OR, USA"><meta name="citation_author" content="Aaron Liston"><meta name="citation_author_institution" content="Department of Botany &amp; Plant Pathology, Oregon State University, Corvallis, OR, USA">
        <meta name="description" content="Milkweeds (Asclepias) are used in wide-ranging studies including floral development, pollination biology, plant-insect interactions and co-evolution, secondary metabolite chemistry, and rapid diversification. We present a transcriptome and draft nuclear genome assembly of the common milkweed, Asclepias syriaca. This reconstruction of the nuclear genome is augmented by linkage group information, adding to existing chloroplast and mitochondrial genomic resources for this member of the Apocynaceae subfamily Asclepiadoideae. The genome was sequenced to 80.4× depth and the draft assembly contains 54,266 scaffolds ≥1 kbp, with N50 = 3,415 bp, representing 37% (156.6 Mbp) of the estimated 420 Mbp genome. A total of 14,474 protein-coding genes were identified based on transcript evidence, closely related proteins, and ab initio models, and 95% of genes were annotated. A large proportion of gene space is represented in the assembly, with 96.7% of Asclepias transcripts, 88.4% of transcripts from the related genus Calotropis, and 90.6% of proteins from Coffea mapping to the assembly. Scaffolds covering 75 Mbp of the Asclepias assembly formed 11 linkage groups. Comparisons of these groups with pseudochromosomes in Coffea found that six chromosomes show consistent stability in gene content, while one may have a long history of fragmentation and rearrangement. The progesterone 5β-reductase gene family, a key component of cardenolide production, is likely reduced in Asclepias relative to other Apocynaceae. The genome and transcriptome of common milkweed provide a rich resource for future studies of the ecology and evolution of a charismatic plant family.">

<script>
    var campaign_keywords = ['utm_source', 'utm_medium', 'utm_campaign', 'utm_content', 'utm_term'];
    var kw = '';
    var lastUtms = {};
    var firstUtms = {};
    var allUtms = {};

    function campaignParams() {
        var index;
        for (index = 0; index < campaign_keywords.length; ++index) {
            kw = getQueryParam(document.URL, campaign_keywords[index]);
            if (kw.length) {
                lastUtms[campaign_keywords[index] + '-last'] = kw;
                firstUtms[campaign_keywords[index] + '-first'] = kw;
                allUtms[campaign_keywords[index] + '-all'] = kw;
            }
        }
    }

    function updatePreregCookie(preregCookie, firstUtmKey) {
        var utmVal = firstUtms[firstUtmKey];
        if (utmVal) {
            var existingPreregCampaign = $.cookie(preregCookie);
            var appendPreregCampaign;
            if (!existingPreregCampaign) {
                appendPreregCampaign = utmVal;
            } else {
                appendPreregCampaign = existingPreregCampaign + ',' + utmVal;

            }
            $.cookie(preregCookie, appendPreregCampaign, {expires: 365, path: "/"});
        }
    }

    function getQueryParam(url, param) {
        // Expects a raw URL
        param = param.replace(/[[]/, "\[").replace(/[]]/, "\]");
        var regexS = "[\?&]" + param + "=([^&#]*)",
                regex = new RegExp( regexS ),
                results = regex.exec(url);
        if (results === null || (results && typeof(results[1]) !== 'string' && results[1].length)) {
            return '';
        } else {
            return decodeURIComponent(results[1]).replace(/\W/gi, ' ');
        }
    }

    function articlePageEvent() {
        var articleContainer = $('.publication-jsondata');
        if (articleContainer.length) {
            var data = articleContainer.data('publication-meta');

            // Must be public
            if (data.publicationSubjects.length) {

                var eventName = 'Viewed-article';
                var preprint = data.preprint;
                if (preprint) {
                    eventName = 'Viewed-preprint';
                }

                data['ip-hash'] = 'b840d58e443ff19b92460a9e03ca728f';
                mixpanel.track(eventName, data);
            }
        }
    }

    function sectionListViewEvent() {
            }
</script>

<div>
<h2>Abstract</h2>
<div class="abstract" itemprop="description">
<p>Milkweeds (<i>Asclepias</i>) are used in wide-ranging studies including floral development, pollination biology, plant-insect interactions and co-evolution, secondary metabolite chemistry, and rapid diversification. We present a transcriptome and draft nuclear genome assembly of the common milkweed, <i>Asclepias syriaca</i>. This reconstruction of the nuclear genome is augmented by linkage group information, adding to existing chloroplast and mitochondrial genomic resources for this member of the Apocynaceae subfamily Asclepiadoideae. The genome was sequenced to 80.4× depth and the draft assembly contains 54,266 scaffolds ≥1 kbp, with N50 = 3,415 bp, representing 37% (156.6 Mbp) of the estimated 420 Mbp genome. A total of 14,474 protein-coding genes were identified based on transcript evidence, closely related proteins, and ab initio models, and 95% of genes were annotated. A large proportion of gene space is represented in the assembly, with 96.7% of <i>Asclepias</i> transcripts, 88.4% of transcripts from the related genus <i>Calotropis</i>, and 90.6% of proteins from <i>Coffea</i> mapping to the assembly. Scaffolds covering 75 Mbp of the <i>Asclepias</i> assembly formed 11 linkage groups. Comparisons of these groups with pseudochromosomes in <i>Coffea</i> found that six chromosomes show consistent stability in gene content, while one may have a long history of fragmentation and rearrangement. The progesterone 5β-reductase gene family, a key component of cardenolide production, is likely reduced in <i>Asclepias</i> relative to other Apocynaceae. The genome and transcriptome of common milkweed provide a rich resource for future studies of the ecology and evolution of a charismatic plant family.</p>
</div>
</div></header><main><div class="body" lang="en">
<section class="sec" id="intro">
<h2 class="heading">Introduction</h2>
<p id="p-1">The development of genomic resources for an ever-increasing portion of the diversity of life is benefiting every field of biology in myriad ways. The decreasing cost of sequencing and the continual development of bioinformatic tools are allowing even single labs and small collaborations to produce genomic content that is beneficial and accessible to the wider research community. This study presents such a resource, including a draft genome assembly of a species in the milkweed genus <i>Asclepias</i> (Apocynaceae).</p>
<p id="p-2"><i>Asclepias</i> sensu stricto is made up of about 130 species in North and South America (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1600%2F036364411X605010" title="Phylogenetic relationships of Asclepias (Apocynaceae) inferred from non-coding chloroplast DNA sequences" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-33">Fishbein et al., 2011</a>). The genus in the Americas is found in a wide range of habitats, from deserts to swamps, plains to shaded forests, and may represent a rapid ecological expansion (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1002%2Fajb2.1062" title="Evolution at the tips: Asclepias phylogenomics and new perspectives on leaf surfaces" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-34">Fishbein et al., 2018</a>). The common milkweed, <i>Asclepias syriaca</i> L. (<a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-1" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-1">Fig. 1</a>), inhabits wide swaths of eastern North America, westward to Kansas, and northward to Canada (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.2307%2F2394652" title="The North American species of Asclepias L" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-131">Woodson, 1954</a>). It is well known for the milky latex exuded when injured, showy inflorescences, and pods filled with seeds tufted with fine hairs.</p>
<figure class="fig" itemprop="image" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject" id="fig-1"><div class="image-container"><a href="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-1-2x.jpg" title="View the full image" class="fresco" data-fresco-caption="Figure 1: Asclepias syriaca inflorescence." data-fresco-group="figure" data-fresco-options="fit: 'width', ui: 'outside', thumbnails: false, loop: true, position: true, overflow: true, preload: false"><img class="graphic" src="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-1-1x.jpg" itemprop="contentUrl" sizes="(min-width: 1200px) 581px, (max-width: 1199px) and (min-width: 980px) 462px, (max-width: 979px) and (min-width: 768px) 347px, (max-width: 767px) calc(100vw - 50px)" srcset="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-1-2x.jpg 1200w, https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-1-1x.jpg 600w, https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-1-small.jpg 355w" data-image-id="fig-1" alt="Asclepias syriaca inflorescence." data-full="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-1-full.png" data-thumb="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-1-thumb.jpg" data-original="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-1.png" data-image-type="figure" data-jats-mimetype="image" data-jats-mime-subtype="png" width="600" height="452"></a></div>
<figcaption itemprop="description">
<h3 class="heading">
<span class="caption-label">Figure 1: </span><i>Asclepias syriaca</i> inflorescence.</h3>
<span class="p">An inflorescence of <i>A. syriaca</i>. Note the floral coronas (white to light pink) surrounding each central gynostegium. Photo credit: Mark Fishbein.</span>
<div class="figcaption-footer">
<div class="article-image-download"><a href="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-1-full.png" class="btn btn-mini" download="peerj-7649-fig-1.png" itemprop="url"><i class="icon-large icon-picture"> </i> Download full-size image</a></div>
<div class="object-id article-component-doi">DOI: <a href="https://doi.org/10.7717/peerj.7649/fig-1" data-toggle="tooltip" title="Cite this object using this DOI">10.7717/peerj.7649/fig-1</a>
</div>
</div></figcaption></figure>
<p id="p-4">As members of Apocynaceae subfamily Asclepiadoideae, <i>Asclepias</i> species possess floral architectures unique among plants, including floral coronas and a central gynostegium composed of the unified stamens and pistil (<a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-1" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-1">Fig. 1</a>). Most <i>Asclepias</i> species are nearly or entirely self-incompatible (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1146%2Fannurev.es.25.110194.002231" title="Ecology and evolution of reproduction in milkweeds" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-133">Wyatt &amp; Broyles, 1994</a>), and their pollen is packaged into masses, pollinia, which are transferred as a unit from one flower to another. This usually allows a single successful pollination event to fertilize all of the ovules in an ovary, resulting in full-sibling families in each fruit (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Pollen%20compatibility%20in%20Asclepias%20syriaca&amp;author=Sparrow&amp;publication_year=1948" title="Pollen compatibility in Asclepias syriaca" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-111">Sparrow &amp; Pearson, 1948</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Reproductive%20biology%20of%20milkweeds%20(Asclepias):%20Recent%20advances&amp;author=Wyatt&amp;publication_year=1990" title="Reproductive biology of milkweeds (Asclepias): Recent advances" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-132">Wyatt &amp; Broyles, 1990</a>). The unusual features of <i>Asclepias</i> pollination and floral architecture have positioned it as a model in studies of angiosperm reproductive biology (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.2307%2F2409329" title="Paternity analysis in a natural population of Asclepias exaltata: multiple paternity, functional gender, and the “pollen-donation hypothesis”" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-20">Broyles &amp; Wyatt, 1990</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Reproductive%20biology%20of%20milkweeds%20(Asclepias):%20Recent%20advances&amp;author=Wyatt&amp;publication_year=1990" title="Reproductive biology of milkweeds (Asclepias): Recent advances" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-132">Wyatt &amp; Broyles, 1990</a>, <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1146%2Fannurev.es.25.110194.002231" title="Ecology and evolution of reproduction in milkweeds" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-133">1994</a>), floral development (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Angiosperm%20floral%20evolution:%20morphological%20developmental%20framework.%20Advances%20in%20Botanical%20Research&amp;author=Endress&amp;publication_year=2006" title="Angiosperm floral evolution: morphological developmental framework. Advances in Botanical Research" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-30">Endress, 2006</a>, <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Faob%2Fmcv119" title="Development and evolution of extreme synorganization in angiosperm flowers and diversity: a comparison of Apocynaceae and Orchidaceae" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-31">2015</a>), selection on floral characters and prezygotic reproductive isolation (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fhdy.1997.178" title="Selection on reproductive characters: floral morphology in Asclepias syriaca" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-85">Morgan &amp; Schoen, 1997</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.3732%2Fajb.1600328" title="Floral function: effects of traits on pollinators, male and female pollination success, and female fitness across three species of milkweeds (Asclepias)" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-66">La Rosa &amp; Conner, 2017</a>), and floral display evolution (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.2307%2F2424201" title="Adaptive design of the floral display in Asclepias syriaca L" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-130">Willson &amp; Rathcke, 1974</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.2307%2F1940126" title="Energetic constraints and adaptive significance of the floral display of a forest milkweed" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-22">Chaplin &amp; Walker, 1982</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fj.1558-5646.1996.tb03607.x" title="Evolution of inflorescence design: theory and data" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-35">Fishbein &amp; Venable, 1996</a>).</p>
<p id="p-5">Milkweeds produce an array of potent secondary compounds, including cardiac glycosides (specifically cardenolides). Some herbivores possess defenses to avoid or tolerate these compounds, including the monarch butterfly, <i>Danaus plexippus</i>. Monarch caterpillars are able to sequester cardenolides from <i>Asclepias</i> to use for their own defense, and <i>Asclepias</i> species are an essential host for monarchs (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1073%2Fpnas.57.4.893" title="Plant poisons in a terrestrial food chain" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-19">Brower, Van Brower &amp; Corvino, 1967</a>). The variation within and among <i>Asclepias</i> species in types of and investments in defensive compounds and structures has led to studies of defensive trait evolution (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Plant%20defense%20syndromes&amp;author=Agrawal&amp;publication_year=2006" title="Plant defense syndromes" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-3">Agrawal &amp; Fishbein, 2006</a>, <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1073%2Fpnas.0802368105" title="Phylogenetic escalation and decline of plant defense strategies" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-4">2008</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1890%2F08-1895.1" title="Cardenolides, induced responses, and interactions between above- and belowground herbivores of milkweed (Asclepias spp.)" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-100">Rasmann et al., 2009</a>, <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fj.1365-2745.2010.01713.x" title="Direct and indirect root defences of milkweed (Asclepias syriaca): trophic cascades, trade-offs and novel methods for studying subterranean herbivory" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-101">2011</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fj.1469-8137.2011.04049.x" title="Toxic cardenolides: chemical ecology and coevolution of specialized plant-herbivore interactions" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-5">Agrawal et al., 2012</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1002%2Fajb2.1062" title="Evolution at the tips: Asclepias phylogenomics and new perspectives on leaf surfaces" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-34">Fishbein et al., 2018</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fnph.15061" title="Evolution of pyrrolizidine alkaloid biosynthesis in Apocynaceae: revisiting the defence de-escalation hypothesis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-76">Livshultz et al., 2018</a>), plant-herbivore ecological interactions (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1073%2Fpnas.57.4.893" title="Plant poisons in a terrestrial food chain" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-19">Brower, Van Brower &amp; Corvino, 1967</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1126%2Fscience.177.4047.426" title="Variation in cardiac glycoside content of Monarch butterflies from natural populations in eastern North America" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-18">Brower et al., 1972</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1007%2FBF00987690" title="Effect of gross cardiac glycoside content of seeds of common milkweed, Asclepias syriaca, on cardiac glycoside uptake by the milkweed bug Oncopeltus fasciatus" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-124">Vaughan, 1979</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1890%2F03-0622" title="Community-wide impacts of herbivore-induced plant responses in milkweed (Asclepias syriaca)" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-123">Van Zandt &amp; Agrawal, 2004</a>), and plant-herbivore co-evolution (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1046%2Fj.1365-2745.2003.00831.x" title="Ecological play in the coevolutionary theatre: genetic and environmental determinants of attack by a specialist weevil on milkweed" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-6">Agrawal &amp; Van Zandt, 2003</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fmolbev%2Fmsg240" title="Molecular adaptation of Chrysochus leaf beetles to toxic compounds in their food plants" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-67">Labeyrie &amp; Dobler, 2004</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Natural%20selection%20on%20common%20milkweed%20(Asclepias%20syriaca)%20by%20a%20community%20of%20specialized%20insect%20herbivores&amp;author=Agrawal&amp;publication_year=2005" title="Natural selection on common milkweed (Asclepias syriaca) by a community of specialized insect herbivores" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-2">Agrawal, 2005</a>).</p>
<p id="p-6">A few genomic resources have been developed for <i>Asclepias</i> and other Apocynaceae. The chloroplast and mitochondrial genomes of <i>Asclepias syriaca</i> have been sequenced (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2F1471-2164-12-211" title="Building a model: developing genomic resources for common milkweed (Asclepias syriaca) with low coverage genome sequencing" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-114">Straub et al., 2011</a>, <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fgbe%2Fevt140" title="Horizontal transfer of DNA from the mitochondrial to the plastid genome and its subsequent evolution in milkweeds (Apocynaceae)" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-113">2013</a>), and flow cytometry estimates place the nuclear genome size of <i>Asclepias syriaca</i> at 420 Mbp (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fj.1469-8137.2012.04370.x" title="A multivariate analysis of variation in genome size and endoreduplication in angiosperms reveals strong phylogenetic signal and association with phenotypic traits" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-12">Bainard et al., 2012</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Faob%2Fmcs222" title="New reports of nuclear DNA content for 407 vascular plant taxa from the United States" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-11">Bai et al., 2012</a>). <i>Asclepias</i> is not the first member of Apocynaceae to receive nuclear genome sequencing. Genomic sequencing and assembly of <i>Catharanthus roseus</i> (subfamily Rauvolfioideae) was performed by <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Ftpj.12827" title="Genome-guided investigation of plant natural product biosynthesis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-58">Kellner et al. (2015)</a> to investigate the production of medicinal compounds (<a class="xref xref-table" href="#table-1" data-jats-ref-type="table" data-jats-rid="table-1">Table 1</a>). <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fsrep33782" title="The nuclear genome of Rhazya stricta and the evolution of alkaloid diversity in a medically relevant clade of Apocynaceae" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-104">Sabir et al. (2016)</a> assembled the genome of <i>Rhazya stricta</i> (Rauvolfioideae) and <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1534%2Fg3.117.300331" title="Genome assembly and annotation of the medicinal plant Calotropis gigantea, a producer of anticancer and antimalarial cardenolides" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-48">Hoopes et al. (2018)</a> assembled the <i>Calotropis gigantea</i> (Asclepiadoideae) genome, investigating alkaloid diversity and cardenolide production, respectively (<a class="xref xref-table" href="#table-1" data-jats-ref-type="table" data-jats-rid="table-1">Table 1</a>).</p>
<figure class="table-wrap" id="table-1"><div class="caption">

<p id="p-10">The transcriptomes of several species of Apocynaceae have also been released as part of broader investigations into medicinally important plants, particularly those producing monoterpene indole alkaloids, including <i>Tabernaemontana elegans</i> (Rauvolfioideae), <i>Rauvolfia serpentina</i> (Rauvolfioideae), <i>Rhazya stricta</i>, and <i>Catharanthus roseus</i> (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="http://medicinalplantgenomics.msu.edu/final_version_release_info.shtml" title="Release of the medicinal plant consortium transcriptome resources" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-83">Medicinal Plant Consortium, 2011</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0052506" title="Development of transcriptomic resources for interrogating the biosynthesis of monoterpene indole alkaloids in medicinal plant species" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-40">Góngora-Castillo et al., 2012a</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.jbiotec.2013.04.004" title="Transcriptome analysis based on next-generation sequencing of non-model plants producing specialized metabolites of biotechnological interest" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-134">Xiao et al., 2013</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2F1471-2229-14-2" title="The temporal foliar transcriptome of the perennial C3 desert plant Rhazya stricta in its natural environment" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-135">Yates et al., 2014</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2F1471-2164-15-405" title="Complete sequences of organelle genomes from the medicinal plant Rhazya stricta (Apocynaceae) and contrasting patterns of mitochondrial genome evolution across asterids" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-91">Park et al., 2014</a>). The transcriptome of <i>Calotropis procera</i> has also been investigated (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0119328" title="Cysteine protease profiles of the medicinal plant Calotropis procera R. Br. revealed by de novo transcriptome analysis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-65">Kwon et al., 2015</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fsrep34464" title="Transcriptome and metabolite analysis reveal candidate genes of the cardiac glycoside biosynthetic pathway from Calotropis procera" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-90">Pandey et al., 2016</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1534%2Fg3.117.300331" title="Genome assembly and annotation of the medicinal plant Calotropis gigantea, a producer of anticancer and antimalarial cardenolides" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-48">Hoopes et al., 2018</a>).</p>
<p id="p-11">Outside of Apocynaceae the most closely related species to milkweed with a sequenced genome is the diploid ancestor of coffee, <i>Coffea canephora</i> (Rubiaceae; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1126%2Fscience.1255274" title="The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-24">Denoeud et al., 2014</a>). <i>Coffea</i> is in the same order as <i>Asclepias</i>, Gentianales, and <i>Coffea canephora</i> has the same number of chromosomes: <i>x</i> = <i>n</i> = 11, 2<i>n</i> = 22 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1126%2Fscience.1255274" title="The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-24">Denoeud et al., 2014</a>). The <i>Coffea</i> genome assembly is a high-quality reference, with large scaffolds ordered onto pseudochromosomes (scaffolds that have been ordered based on linkage information, as though on a chromosome; <a class="xref xref-table" href="#table-1" data-jats-ref-type="table" data-jats-rid="table-1">Table 1</a>).</p>
<p id="p-12">The genomic assembly of <i>Asclepias syriaca</i> presented here includes a nearly complete representation of gene space, supported by transcriptome evidence. The heterozygosity present in this obligate outcrossing species is used to develop a panel of single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) that can be captured via targeted enrichment, and a set of offspring from the sequenced individual is used to cluster assembled scaffolds into linkage groups. A comparison of linkage groups between <i>Asclepias</i> and <i>Coffea</i> is presented, providing insights into chromosome organization in <i>Asclepias</i>, and chromosomal evolution within Gentianales. Both genome and transcriptome sequences are used to explore gene family evolution, especially as related to cardenolide biosynthesis.</p>
</section>
<section class="sec" id="methods">
<h2 class="heading">Methods</h2>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Tissue preparation and library construction</h3>
<p id="p-13">Leaf tissue of <i>Asclepias syriaca</i> was sampled from a single individual at the Western Illinois University research farm, raised from seed from a wild population in McDonough County, Illinois (40.29622°N, 90.89876°W; Winthrop B. Phippen <i>s.n</i>., OSC 226164, 226165). DNA was extracted from frozen tissue using the FastDNA Spin Kit from MPBiomedicals (Santa Ana, CA, USA) following manufacturer’s protocols, modified by the addition of 40 μl 1% polyvinylpyrrolidone and 10 μl β-mercaptoethanol to the 1,000 μl lysis solution (800 μl CLS-VF + 200 μl PPS) prior to grinding.</p>
<p id="p-14">Aliquots of isolated DNA were sheared with a BioRuptor sonicator (Diagenode Inc., Denville, NJ, USA) at low power for 10 cycles of 30 s on/30 s off. Two libraries were prepared using the Illumina Paired-End DNA Sample Prep Kit (catalog number PE-102-1001; Illumina Inc., San Diego, CA, USA; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Protocol%20for%20whole%20genome%20sequencing%20using%20Solexa%20technology&amp;author=&amp;publication_year=2006" title="Protocol for whole genome sequencing using Solexa technology" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-110">Solexa, Inc, 2006</a>). Ligated fragments were cut from agarose gels centered around 225 and 450 bp, and were amplified through 15 and 14 cycles, respectively, of polymerase chain reaction using Phusion High-Fidelity PCR Master Mix (New England BioLabs, Ipswich, MA, USA) and standard Illumina primers. Cleaned product was submitted for sequencing on an Illumina GAII Sequencer at the Center for Genome Research and Biocomputing (CGRB) at Oregon State University (Corvallis, OR, USA). One lane of the 450 bp library was sequenced with 80 bp paired-end reads, and five lanes of the 225 bp library were sequenced with 120 bp paired-end reads.</p>
<p id="p-15">Frozen tissue of the sequenced individual was sent to GlobalBiologics, LLC (Columbia, MO, USA) for DNA extraction and production of mate-pair libraries using the Illumina Mate Pair Library v2 protocol with average insert sizes of 2,750 and 3,500 bp, and indexed with unique barcode sequences (Bioo Scientific, Austin, TX, USA). The 2,750 bp library was sequenced with 101 bp paired-end reads on an Illumina HiSeq 2000 sequencer at the CGRB, on the same lane as two other samples from unrelated projects. The 3,500 bp library was sequenced on an Illumina MiSeq at Oregon Health and Science University (Portland, OR, USA) with 33 bp paired-end reads (<a class="xref xref-table" href="#table-2" data-jats-ref-type="table" data-jats-rid="table-2">Table 2</a>). Purified DNA of that same individual was also provided to the CGRB for production of a mate-pair library using the Illumina Nextera protocol with an average insert size of 2,000 bp. This library was sequenced with 76 bp paired-end reads on an Illumina MiSeq at the CGRB, along with 14 other samples from unrelated projects (<a class="xref xref-table" href="#table-2" data-jats-ref-type="table" data-jats-rid="table-2">Table 2</a>).</p>
