</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Genomic read processing</h3>
<p id="p-18">Read pools were evaluated for quality parameters using FastQC (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc/" title="FastQC–a quality control tool for high throughput sequence data" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-9">Andrews, 2010</a>). Pairs of reads properly mapping to the <i>Asclepias</i> chloroplast or mitochondria, with three or fewer mismatches between the target and query, were filtered out using Bowtie 2 v. 2.1.0 (scoring parameter “--score-min L,-6,0”), samtools v. 0.1.18, and bamtools v. 2.3.0 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtp352" title="The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-72">Li et al., 2009</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fnmeth.1923" title="Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-68">Langmead &amp; Salzberg, 2012</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://github.com/pezmaster31/bamtools" title="bamtools" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-14">Barnett et al., 2013</a>). Portions of reads matching the Illumina adapter sequences were removed with Trimmomatic v. 0.30 and the “ILLUMINACLIP:TruSeq2-PE.fa:2:30:10” option (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtu170" title="Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-17">Bolger, Lohse &amp; Usadel, 2014</a>). Duplicate read pairs from the same library were removed using the custom script fastq_collapse.py (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://github.com/kweitemier/fastq_collapse" title="fastq_collapse.py" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-126">Weitemier, 2014</a>). Paired-end read pairs with sequences that overlapped by ≥7 bp sharing ≥90% identity were merged using the program FLASH v. 1.2.6 (parameters “-m 7 -M 80 -x 0.10”) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtr507" title="FLASH: fast length adjustment of short reads to improve genome assemblies" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-80">Magoč &amp; Salzberg, 2011</a>). The 3′ and 5′ ends of reads were then trimmed of any bases with a Phred quality score below 30, and any remaining reads less than 30 bp were removed using Trimmomatic command “LEADING:30 TRAILING:30 MINLEN:30.”</p>
<p id="p-19">Summary statistics were calculated using a <i>k</i>-mer distribution plot of reads from the 225 bp insert library after removing chloroplast and mitochondrial reads, but prior to joining with FLASH. <i>K</i>-mers of 17 bp were counted using BBTools script kmercountexact.sh, and estimates of genome size and heterozygosity were calculated using the program gce (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="http://arxiv.org/abs/1308.2012v1" title="Estimation of genomic characteristics by analyzing k-mer frequency in de novo genome projects" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-75">Liu et al., 2013</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://sourceforge.net/projects/bbmap/" title="BBTools" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-21">Bushnell &amp; Rood, 2015</a>).</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">RNA-seq library preparation, sequencing, and assembly</h3>
<p id="p-20">Total RNA was extracted from the individual used for genome sequencing from leaves and buds separately, by homogenizing approximately 200 mg of fresh frozen tissue on dry ice in a Fast-Prep-24 bead mill. Cold extraction buffer (1.5 ml of 3M LiCl/8M urea; 1% PVP K-60; 0.1M dithiothreitol; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1007%2FBF02773357" title="Total RNA isolation from Picea mariana dry seed" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-115">Tai, Pelletier &amp; Beardmore, 2004</a>) was added to the ground tissue. Tissue was then homogenized and cellular debris pelleted at 200<i>×g</i> for 10 min at 4 °C. Supernatant was incubated at 4 °C overnight. RNA was pelleted by centrifugation (20,000<i>×g</i> for 30 min at 4 °C) and cleaned using a ZR Plant RNA MiniPrep kit (Zymo Research, Irvine, CA, USA). The integrity of the extracted RNA was assessed using an Agilent 2100 Bioanalyzer (Agilent Technologies, Santa Clara, CA, USA); extractions from both tissues showed RIN values greater than 8.0. For each tissue type, an RNA-seq library was prepared using the Illumina RNA-Seq TruSeq kit v. 2.0 with the modifications of <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fnar%2Fgkp596" title="Transcriptome analysis by strand-specific sequencing of complementary DNA" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-92">Parkhomchuk et al. (2009)</a> to allow strand-specific sequencing by dUTP incorporation.</p>
<p id="p-21">Libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2000 at the CGRB to yield 101 bp single-end reads. Before further analysis, reads that did not pass the Illumina chastity and purity filters were removed. Trimmomatic 0.20 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtu170" title="Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-17">Bolger, Lohse &amp; Usadel, 2014</a>) was used to trim the final base of each read, leading and trailing bases with quality scores below Q20, and all following bases if a sliding window of five bp did not have an average quality of at least Q30. Reads shorter than 36 bp after trimming were excluded (Trimmomatic command “CROP:100 LEADING:20 TRAILING:20 SLIDINGWINDOW:5:30 MINLEN:36”).</p>
<p id="p-22">Transcripts were assembled de novo using Trinity (Release 2013-08-14) (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fnbt.1883" title="Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-42">Grabherr et al., 2011</a>) for bud and leaf reads separately, as well as combined into a single data set using default settings, except for using a minimum contig length of 101 bp. The same settings were also used to assemble RNA-seq data from leaf tissue of the same <i>Asclepias syriaca</i> individual from a library made using ribosomal RNA subtraction (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fgbe%2Fevt140" title="Horizontal transfer of DNA from the mitochondrial to the plastid genome and its subsequent evolution in milkweeds (Apocynaceae)" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-113">Straub et al., 2013</a>). Best-scoring open reading frames (ORFs) were determined for each library based on attributes including length, reading frame, and nucleotide composition using the TransDecoder utility provided with Trinity (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fnprot.2013.084" title="De novo transcript sequence reconstruction from RNA-seq using the Trinity platform for reference generation and analysis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-46">Haas et al., 2013</a>). Transcripts were annotated using Mercator (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fpce.12231" title="Mercator: a fast and simple web server for genome scale functional annotation of plant sequence data" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-77">Lohse et al., 2014</a>) and TRAPID (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2Fgb-2013-14-12-r134" title="TRAPID: an efficient online tool for the functional and comparative analysis of de novoRNA-Seq transcriptomes" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-121">Van Bel et al., 2013</a>). The Mercator analysis was conducted with default options, with the exception of not allowing multiple bin assignments. Therefore multiple databases, including UniProt, were used in annotation. The TRAPID annotations were based on the Plaza 2.5 reference database (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1104%2Fpp.111.189514" title="Dissecting plant genomes with the PLAZA comparative genomics platform" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-122">Van Bel et al., 2012</a>), and the similarity search was restricted to the eudicot clade with an <i>E</i>-value cutoff of 10<i>e<sup>−</sup></i><sup>5</sup>. Functional annotations were added to transcripts based on both gene family and best database hit.</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Comparative transcriptome and gene family evolution analyses in Apocynaceae</h3>
<p id="p-23">For a comparative analysis, transcriptomes were obtained for five other species of Apocynaceae. <i>Catharanthus roseus</i> and <i>Rauvolfia serpentina</i> transcriptomes were downloaded from the Medicinal Plant Genomics Resource project database (<a class="uri" href="http://medicinalplantgenomics.msu.edu">http://medicinalplantgenomics.msu.edu</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Genomic%20approaches%20for%20interrogating%20the%20biochemistry%20of%20medicinal%20plant%20species.%20Methods%20in%20Enzymology&amp;author=G%C3%B3ngora-Castillo&amp;publication_year=2012b" title="Genomic approaches for interrogating the biochemistry of medicinal plant species. Methods in Enzymology" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-41">Góngora-Castillo et al., 2012b</a>), the <i>Rhazya stricta</i> (GenBank <a class="uri" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/GAMW01000000">GAMW01000000</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2F1471-2229-14-2" title="The temporal foliar transcriptome of the perennial C3 desert plant Rhazya stricta in its natural environment" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-135">Yates et al., 2014</a>) and <i>Calotropis procera</i> (GenBank <a class="uri" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/GBHG01000000">GBHG01000000</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0119328" title="Cysteine protease profiles of the medicinal plant Calotropis procera R. Br. revealed by de novo transcriptome analysis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-65">Kwon et al., 2015</a>) transcriptomes were downloaded from National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI), and the <i>Tabernaemontana elegans</i> transcriptome was downloaded from the PhytoMetaSyn Project database (<a class="uri" href="https://bioinformatics.tugraz.at/phytometasyn">https://bioinformatics.tugraz.at/phytometasyn</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.jbiotec.2013.04.004" title="Transcriptome analysis based on next-generation sequencing of non-model plants producing specialized metabolites of biotechnological interest" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-134">Xiao et al., 2013</a>). All transcriptomes, including that of <i>Asclepias syriaca</i>, were checked for duplicate transcripts, and the duplicates removed using the Dedupe tool in BBMap (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://sourceforge.net/projects/bbmap/" title="BBTools" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-21">Bushnell &amp; Rood, 2015</a>). Transcriptomes were checked for completeness using BUSCO v. 1.22 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtv351" title="BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-107">Simão et al., 2015</a>). Transcripts of all species were assigned to reference gene families using TRAPID. Reference gene family assignments were obtained from two high quality genomes, <i>Coffea canephora</i> (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1126%2Fscience.1255274" title="The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-24">Denoeud et al., 2014</a>) and <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (PLAZA v. 2.5; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1105%2Ftpc.109.071506" title="PLAZA: a comparative genomics resource to study gene and genome evolution in plants" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-95">Proost et al., 2009</a>).</p>
<p id="p-24">A phylogenetic framework for comparative analysis was produced using published evolutionary relationships and divergence times in Apocynaceae (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1002%2Fajb2.1062" title="Evolution at the tips: Asclepias phylogenomics and new perspectives on leaf surfaces" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-34">Fishbein et al., 2018</a>). The timings of the <i>Coffea</i> split from Apocynaceae and the <i>Vitis</i> split from Gentianales were based on the estimates of <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0126690" title="A revised time tree of the Asterids: establishing a temporal framework for evolutionary studies of the coffee family (Rubiaceae)" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-129">Wikström et al. (2015)</a>. In order to examine changes in gene family sizes across Apocynaceae transcriptomes, BadiRate v. 1.35 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtr623" title="BadiRate: estimating family turnover rates by likelihood-based methods" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-74">Librado, Vieira &amp; Rozas, 2012</a>) was run using the birth-death-innovation stochastic model with a free rate branch model where each branch can have a different gene turn-over rate. Gains and losses were inferred using Wagner (ordered) parsimony (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fsysbio%2F18.1.1" title="Quantitative phyletics and the evolution of anurans" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-62">Kluge &amp; Farris, 1969</a>).</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Genomic sequence assembly</h3>
<p id="p-25">Processed read-pairs were assembled into contigs using Platanus v. 1.2.1 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1101%2Fgr.170720.113" title="Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-56">Kajitani et al., 2014</a>). Platanus is designed to assemble highly heterozygous diploid genomes, and initially uses several <i>k</i>-mer sizes during assembly. <i>Asclepias</i> reads were assembled with an initial <i>k</i>-mer size of 25 bp with a <i>k</i>-mer step increase of 10 bp up to a maximum <i>k</i>-mer of 110 bp. As part of the expectation for heterozygous assembly, Platanus can merge contigs sharing high identity. We allowed contigs sharing 85% identity to be merged (assembly parameters “-k 25 -u 0.15”).</p>
<p id="p-26">Scaffolding was performed with Platanus, setting the paired-end reads as “inward pointing” reads and the mate-pair reads as “outward pointing” reads. Reads were mapped to scaffolds using an initial seed size of 21 bp, one link between contigs was sufficient to align them into a scaffold, and scaffolds sharing 85% identity could be merged (scaffolding parameters “-s 21 -l 1 -u 0.15”).</p>
<p id="p-27">Gaps between scaffolds were closed via local alignment and assembly of reads around the gaps using Platanus. An initial seed size of 21 bp was used to include reads in the mapping around a gap, and a minimum overlap of 21 bp between the newly assembled filler contig and the edges of the scaffold was required to use that contig to fill the gap (gap close parameters “-s 21 -k 21 -vd 21 -vo 21”).</p>
<p id="p-28">Transcripts were mapped to <i>Asclepias</i> scaffolds ≥1 kbp using BLAT v. 32×1; one or more transcripts spanning multiple scaffolds were used to merge those scaffolds (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1101%2Fgr.229202" title="BLAT—the BLAST-like alignment tool" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-61">Kent, 2002</a>). This was performed with the program Scubat (<a class="uri" href="https://github.com/elswob/SCUBAT">https://github.com/elswob/SCUBAT</a>; accessed December 17, 2015) modified so that scaffolds would not be clipped when joined by cap3 v. 02/10/15 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1101%2Fgr.9.9.868" title="CAP3: a DNA sequence assembly program" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-50">Huang &amp; Madan, 1999</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=GNU%20Parallel:%20The%20command-line%20power%20tool&amp;author=Tange&amp;publication_year=2011" title="GNU Parallel: The command-line power tool" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-116">Tange, 2011</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Unearthing%20the%20genome%20of%20Lumbricus%20rubellus&amp;author=Elsworth&amp;publication_year=2012" title="Unearthing the genome of Lumbricus rubellus" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-28">Elsworth, 2012</a>).</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Contaminant removal</h3>
<p id="p-29">Merged scaffolds were compared against a genomic database of potentially contaminating organisms with the program DeconSeq standalone v. 0.4.3 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0017288" title="Fast identification and removal of sequence contamination from genomic and metagenomic datasets" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-105">Schmieder &amp; Edwards, 2011</a>). Contaminant databases were downloaded from the DeconSeq website representing bacteria, archaea, viruses, 18S rRNA, zebrafish, mouse, and several human genomes (<a class="uri" href="http://deconseq.sourceforge.net">http://deconseq.sourceforge.net</a>; accessed January 20, 2016). Fungal genomes were obtained from the NCBI including <i>Alternaria arborescens</i> accession AIIC01, <i>Aspergillus fumigatus</i> AAHF01, <i>Bipolaris maydis</i> AIHU01, <i>Botrytis cinerea</i> assembly <a class="uri" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/assembly/GCA_000832945.1">GCA_000832945.1</a>, <i>Cladosprium sphaerospermum</i> AIIA02, <i>Fomitopsis pinicola</i> AEHC02, <i>Fusarium oxysporum</i> AAXH01, <i>Galerina marginata</i> AYUM01, <i>Hypoxylon</i> sp. JYCQ01, <i>Penicillium expansum</i> AYHP01, <i>Rhodotorula graminis</i> JTAO01, <i>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</i> assembly <a class="uri" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/assembly/GCA_000146045.2">GCA_000146045.2</a>, and <i>Trichoderma reesei</i> AAIL02 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=The%20yeast%20genome%20directory&amp;author=Goffeau&amp;publication_year=1997" title="The yeast genome directory" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-39">Goffeau et al., 1997</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fnature04332" title="Genomic sequence of the pathogenic and allergenic filamentous fungus Aspergillus fumigatus" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-88">Nierman et al., 2005</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fnbt1403" title="Genome sequencing and analysis of the biomass-degrading fungus Trichoderma reesei (syn. Hypocrea jecorina)" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-82">Martinez et al., 2008</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fnature08850" title="Comparative genomics reveals mobile pathogenicity chromosomes in Fusarium" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-79">Ma et al., 2010</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pgen.1002230" title="Genomic analysis of the necrotrophic fungal pathogens Sclerotinia sclerotiorum and Botrytis cinerea" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-8">Amselem et al., 2011</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2F1471-2164-13-171" title="Genomic characterization of the conditionally dispensable chromosome in Alternaria arborescens provides evidence for horizontal gene transfer" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-49">Hu et al., 2012</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.ppat.1003037" title="Diverse lifestyles and strategies of plant pathogenesis encoded in the genomes of eighteen Dothideomycetes fungi" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-89">Ohm et al., 2012</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1128%2FEC.00081-12" title="Sequencing of Cladosporium sphaerospermum, a Dematiaceous fungus isolated from blood culture" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-87">Ng et al., 2012</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1126%2Fscience.1221748" title="The Paleozoic origin of enzymatic lignin decomposition reconstructed from 31 fungal genomes" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-36">Floudas et al., 2012</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1073%2Fpnas.1400592111" title="Extensive sampling of basidiomycete genomes demonstrates inadequacy of the white-rot/brown-rot paradigm for wood decay fungi" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-103">Riley et al., 2014</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.3389%2Ffmicb.2015.00978" title="Genome sequence of the plant growth promoting endophytic yeast Rhodotorula graminis WP1" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-32">Firrincieli et al., 2015</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1074%2Fjbc.M114.636159" title="Identification of a fungal 1,8-cineole synthase from Hypoxylon sp. with specificity determinants in common with the plant synthases" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-106">Shaw et al., 2015</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1094%2FMPMI-12-14-0398-FI" title="Genomic characterization reveals insights into patulin biosynthesis and pathogenicity in Penicillium species" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-73">Li et al., 2015</a>). The genome of <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (ITAG 2.4) was downloaded from the Sol Genomics Network (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fnature11119" title="The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-119">The Tomato Genome Consortium, 2012</a>). The fungal and <i>Solanum</i> genomes were prepared as DeconSeq databases following the DeconSeq website, including filtering of repeated Ns, removal of duplicate sequences, and indexing with a custom version of BWA released with DeconSeq (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtp698" title="Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-70">Li &amp; Durbin, 2010</a>; <a class="uri" href="http://deconseq.sourceforge.net">http://deconseq.sourceforge.net</a>; accessed January 20, 2016).</p>
<p id="p-30">Genomes obtained from the DeconSeq website and the fungal genomes were used as contaminant databases, the <i>Solanum</i> genome was used as a retain database. Scaffolds matching one of the contaminant genomes with ≥80% identity along ≥80% of the scaffold length were excluded as contaminants. Those scaffolds matching both a contaminating genome and the <i>Solanum</i> genome were retained.</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Gene prediction and annotation</h3>
<p id="p-31">A library of <i>Asclepias</i> repetitive elements was created following guidelines in the MAKER Genome Annotation Pipeline online documentation (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="http://weatherby.genetics.utah.edu/MAKER/wiki/index.php/Repeat_Library_Construction-Basic" title="Repeat library construction-basic" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-53">Jiang, 2015</a>). The program RepeatModeler v. open-1.0.8 was used to integrate the programs RepeatMasker v. open-4.0.5, rmblastn v. 2.2.28, RECON v. 1.08, Tandem Repeats Finder v. 4.07b, and RepeatScout v. 1.0.5 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fnar%2F27.2.573" title="Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-16">Benson, 1999</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1101%2Fgr.88502" title="Automated de novo identification of repeat sequence families in sequenced genomes" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-13">Bao &amp; Eddy, 2002</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbti1018" title="De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-94">Price, Jones &amp; Pevzner, 2005</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/" title="RepeatMasker" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-109">Smit, Hubley &amp; Green, 2015</a>). Repeat models initially missing a repeat annotation were compared, using BLAT, against a library of class I and class II transposable elements acquired from the TESeeker website (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://www3.nd.edu/~teseeker/download.html" title="TESeeker" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-59">Kennedy et al., 2010</a>, <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2F1471-2105-12-130" title="An automated homology-based approach for identifying transposable elements" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-60">2011</a>), and matching sequences provided an annotation. Remaining unannotated models were submitted to the online repeat analysis tool, CENSOR, and provided annotations with a score ≥400% and ≥50% sequence similarity (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2F1471-2105-7-474" title="Annotation, submission and screening of repetitive elements in Repbase: RepbaseSubmitter and censor" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-63">Kohany et al., 2006</a>). A set of proteins from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> was filtered to remove proteins from transposable elements, then compared using BLASTX against the <i>Asclepias</i> repeat models. The program ProtExcluder.pl v. 1.1 then used the BLASTX output to remove repeat models and flanking regions matching <i>Arabidopsis</i> proteins (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2FS0022-2836%2805%2980360-2" title="Basic local alignment search tool" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-7">Altschul et al., 1990</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="http://weatherby.genetics.utah.edu/MAKER/wiki/index.php/Repeat_Library_Construction-Basic" title="Repeat library construction-basic" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-53">Jiang, 2015</a>).</p>
<p id="p-32">The set of scaffolds ≥1 kbp were annotated via the online annotation and curation tool GenSAS v. 4.0 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=GenSAS%E2%80%94an%20online%20integrated%20genome%20sequence%20annotation%20pipeline&amp;author=Lee&amp;publication_year=2011" title="GenSAS—an online integrated genome sequence annotation pipeline" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-69">Lee et al., 2011</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=GenSAS%20v4.0:%20a%20web-based%20platform%20for%20structural%20and%20functional%20genome%20annotation%20and%20curation&amp;author=Humann&amp;publication_year=2016" title="GenSAS v4.0: a web-based platform for structural and functional genome annotation and curation" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-51">Humann et al., 2016</a>), which was used to implement the following tools for repeat masking, transcript and protein mapping, ab initio gene prediction, gene consensus creation, and mapping of <i>Asclepias</i> predicted proteins:<ol class="list" data-jats-list-type="order">
<li class="list-item">
<p id="p-33">Repeats in the assembled sequence were masked via RepeatMasker v. open-4.0.1 using the <i>Asclepias</i> repeat models and using models developed from dicots more broadly (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/" title="RepeatMasker" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-109">Smit, Hubley &amp; Green, 2015</a>).</p>
</li>
<li class="list-item">
<p id="p-34">Multiple datasets were mapped onto <i>Asclepias</i> scaffolds in order to assist with gene prediction. The best-scoring ORFs from assembled <i>Asclepias</i> transcripts were mapped using both BLAT and BLAST (expect &lt;1<i>e</i>-50, 99% identity). Assembled transcripts from <i>Calotropis procera</i> were mapped with BLAT (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0119328" title="Cysteine protease profiles of the medicinal plant Calotropis procera R. Br. revealed by de novo transcriptome analysis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-65">Kwon et al., 2015</a>). Proteins from <i>Coffea canephora</i> were mapped with BLASTX (<i>e</i> &lt; 0.0001; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1126%2Fscience.1255274" title="The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-24">Denoeud et al., 2014</a>). While additional high-quality genomes within Apocynaceae were later released (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fsrep33782" title="The nuclear genome of Rhazya stricta and the evolution of alkaloid diversity in a medically relevant clade of Apocynaceae" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-104">Sabir et al., 2016</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1534%2Fg3.117.300331" title="Genome assembly and annotation of the medicinal plant Calotropis gigantea, a producer of anticancer and antimalarial cardenolides" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-48">Hoopes et al., 2018</a>), they were not available at the time this work was performed.</p>
</li>
<li class="list-item">
<p id="p-35">Genes were predicted using the ab initio tools Augustus v. 3.1.0, SNAP, and PASA (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fnar%2Fgkg770" title="Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-45">Haas et al., 2003</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2F1471-2105-5-59" title="Gene finding in novel genomes" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-64">Korf, 2004</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtn013" title="Using native and syntenically mapped cDNA alignments to improve de novo gene finding" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-112">Stanke et al., 2008</a>). Augustus was run using gene models from <i>Solanum</i>, finding genes on both strands, and allowing partial models; SNAP was run using models from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>. PASA was informed by the best-scoring ORFs from assembled <i>Asclepias</i> transcripts.</p>
</li>
<li class="list-item">
<p id="p-36">Multiple lines of evidence were integrated into a gene consensus using EVidenceModeler (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2Fgb-2008-9-1-r7" title="Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-47">Haas et al., 2008</a>) with the following weights: Augustus, 1; SNAP, 1; <i>Coffea</i> proteins, 5; <i>Asclepias</i> transcripts (BLAST), 7; <i>Asclepias</i> transcripts (BLAT), 7; <i>Calotropis</i> transcripts, 5; PASA, 7. Consensus gene models were then refined using PASA, again informed by <i>Asclepias</i> transcripts.</p>
</li>
<li class="list-item">
<p id="p-37">Predicted proteins were compared to the NCBI plant RefSeq database using BLASTP (expect &lt;1<i>e</i>-4, BLOSUM62 matrix; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Chapter%2018:%20The%20reference%20sequence%20(RefSeq)%20database&amp;author=Pruitt&amp;publication_year=2002" title="Chapter 18: The reference sequence (RefSeq) database" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-97">Pruitt et al., 2002</a>), as well as being mapped against protein sequences from <i>Coffea</i> and <i>Catharanthus roseus</i> (expect &lt;1<i>e</i>-4; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1126%2Fscience.1255274" title="The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-24">Denoeud et al., 2014</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Ftpj.12827" title="Genome-guided investigation of plant natural product biosynthesis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-58">Kellner et al., 2015</a>). Protein families were classified using the InterPro database and InterProScan v. 5.8-49.0 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtu031" title="InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-55">Jones et al., 2014</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fnar%2Fgku1243" title="The InterPro protein families database: the classification resource after 15 years" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-84">Mitchell et al., 2015</a>). Transfer RNAs were identified using tRNAscan-SE v. 1.3.1 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fnar%2F25.5.0955" title="tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-78">Lowe &amp; Eddy, 1997</a>). Additional ORFs were found using the getorf tool from the EMBOSS suite, accepting a minimum of 30 bp (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2FS0168-9525%2800%2902024-2" title="EMBOSS: the European molecular biology open software suite" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-102">Rice, Longden &amp; Bleasby, 2000</a>).</p>
<p id="p-38">Some predicted proteins were missing one or more exons, either because they were fragmented on the ends of scaffolds or, rarely, transcript evidence predicted exons with non-canonical splice sites. The predicted coding sequence produced by GenSas for some of these proteins was out of frame. In these cases the coding sequence was translated under all reading frames and a translation lacking internal stop codons was selected, if available.</p>
</li>
</ol></p>
<p id="p-39">An estimate of the completeness of the assembled gene space was calculated using the program BUSCO v. 1.22 and a set of 956 conserved single copy plant genes (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtv351" title="BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-107">Simão et al., 2015</a>). BUSCO was run independently on the set of coding sequences returned following gene prediction as well as on the assembled scaffolds ≥1 kbp using Augustus gene prediction with <i>Solanum</i> models. Predicted genes from <i>Asclepias</i>, <i>Catharanthus</i>, <i>Coffea</i>, and <i>Vitis</i> (obtained from the PLAZA 3.0 database) were clustered into orthogroups using OrthoFinder v. 0.7.1 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fnature06148" title="The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-118">The French-Italian Public Consortium for Grapevine Genome Characterization, 2007</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2Fs13059-015-0721-2" title="OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-29">Emms &amp; Kelly, 2015</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fnar%2Fgku986" title="PLAZA 3.0: an access point for plant comparative genomics" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-96">Proost et al., 2015</a>).</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Gene analyses</h3>
<p id="p-40">The progesterone 5β-reductase (P5βR) region (PLAZA v. 2.5 gene family HOM000752; InterPro NAD(P)-binding domain IPR016040; Gene Ontology: coenzyme binding <a class="uri" href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/QuickGO/term/GO:0050662">GO:0050662</a>, catalytic activity <a class="uri" href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/QuickGO/term/GO:0003824">GO:0003824</a>) was identified in assembled scaffolds with BLAT (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1101%2Fgr.229202" title="BLAT—the BLAST-like alignment tool" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-61">Kent, 2002</a>), using the P5βR sequences from <i>Asclepias curassavica</i> (<a class="uri" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/ADG56538">ADG56538</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.phytochem.2010.06.004" title="Highly conserved progesterone 5β-reductase genes (P5βR) from 5β-cardenolide-free and 5β-cardenolide-producing angiosperms" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-15">Bauer et al., 2010</a>) and <i>Catharanthus roseus</i> (<a class="uri" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/KJ873882">KJ873882</a>–<a class="uri" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/KJ873887">KJ873887</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.molp.2014.11.005" title="Iridoid synthase activity is common among the plant progesterone 5β-reductase family" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-86">Munkert et al., 2015</a>) as references. A maximum likelihood tree was constructed from peptide sequences of two <i>Asclepias syriaca</i> regions with high identity to P5βR; six <i>Catharanthus</i> P5βR sequences; the <i>Asclepias curassavica</i> sequence; P5βR sequences from <i>Calotropis procera</i> (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0119328" title="Cysteine protease profiles of the medicinal plant Calotropis procera R. Br. revealed by de novo transcriptome analysis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-65">Kwon et al., 2015</a>), <i>Calotropis gigantea</i> (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1534%2Fg3.117.300331" title="Genome assembly and annotation of the medicinal plant Calotropis gigantea, a producer of anticancer and antimalarial cardenolides" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-48">Hoopes et al., 2018</a>), and <i>Rhazya stricta</i> (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fsrep33782" title="The nuclear genome of Rhazya stricta and the evolution of alkaloid diversity in a medically relevant clade of Apocynaceae" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-104">Sabir et al., 2016</a>); sequences from <i>Digitalis purpurea</i> and <i>Digitalis lantata</i> (<a class="uri" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/ACZ66261">ACZ66261</a>, <a class="uri" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/AAS76634">AAS76634</a>), representing P5βR2 and P5βR paralogs, respectively; and a sequence from <i>Picea sitchensis</i> (<a class="uri" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/ABK24388">ABK24388</a>). P5βR sequence alignments were performed using MUSCLE 3.8.425, as implemented in Geneious v. 11.1.5, with a maximum of 10 iterations (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fnar%2Fgkh340" title="MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-26">Edgar, 2004</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbts199" title="Geneious Basic: an integrated and extendable desktop software platform for the organization and analysis of sequence data" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-57">Kearse et al., 2012</a>). The optimal models of amino acid substitution, rate variation among sites, and equilibrium frequencies were inferred using the Akaike and Bayesian information criteria, as implemented in the online tool PhyML 3.0, which was also used to infer trees under those models and calculate aBayes support values (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1080%2F10635150390235520" title="A simple, fast, and accurate algorithm to estimate large phylogenies by maximum likelihood" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-44">Guindon &amp; Gascuel, 2003</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fsysbio%2Fsyq010" title="New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-43">Guindon et al., 2010</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fsysbio%2Fsyr041" title="Survey of branch support methods demonstrates accuracy, power, and robustness of fast likelihood-based approximation schemes" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-10">Anisimova et al., 2011</a>).</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">SNP finding and targeted enrichment probe development</h3>
<p id="p-41">The Platanus genome assembler uses a de Bruijn graph approach for contig assembly (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1101%2Fgr.170720.113" title="Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-56">Kajitani et al., 2014</a>). Certain types of branches in this graph, known as “bubbles,” may be caused by heterozygosity and are saved by the program for use in later assembly stages. Here, saved bubbles were filtered to identify those likely to represent heterozygous sites in low-copy regions of the genome.</p>
<p id="p-42">The program CD-HIT-EST v. 4.5.4 was used to cluster any bubbles sharing ≥90% identity, which were removed, leaving only unique bubbles (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtl158" title="Cd-hit: a fast program for clustering and comparing large sets of protein or nucleotide sequences" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-71">Li &amp; Godzik, 2006</a>). Unique bubbles were mapped against the set of <i>Asclepias</i> scaffolds ≥1 kbp using BLAT at minimum identity thresholds of 90% and 95% (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1101%2Fgr.229202" title="BLAT—the BLAST-like alignment tool" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-61">Kent, 2002</a>). A set of 4,000 SNP probes developed from a preliminary study using a similar approach, but from a different genome assembly, were mapped against the assembly presented here with a 90% identity threshold (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.3732%2Fapps.1400042" title="Hyb-Seq: combining target enrichment and genome skimming for plant phylogenomics" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-128">Weitemier et al., 2014</a>). One appropriate bubble from each scaffold &lt;10 kbp, and up to two bubbles from scaffolds ≥10 kbp, were selected, up to a total of 20,000 bubbles. Bubbles mapping only once within the ≥90% identity mapping analysis were selected first, progressively adding bubbles that either mapped to ≤4 locations in the ≥90% identity mapping or mapped to ≤3 locations in the ≥95% identity mapping. Bubble sequences were trimmed to 80 bp, and centered around the SNP site where possible. Potential SNP probes were further analyzed by MYcroarray (now Arbor BioSciences, Ann Arbor, MI, USA) and excluded if they were predicted to anneal in a solution hybridization reaction to &gt;10 locations within the <i>Asclepias</i> genome at 62.5–65 °C or &gt;2 locations above 65 °C. A total of 20,000 RNA oligos suitable for targeted enrichment, matching 17,684 scaffolds, were produced by MYcroarray. RNA oligo sequences are available in the supplemental data set (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.7267%2FN9M61HDR" title="Supplemental data to A draft genome and transcriptome of common milkweed (Asclepias syriaca) as resources for evolutionary, ecological, and molecular studies in milkweeds and Apocynaceae" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-127">Weitemier, 2017</a>).</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Linkage mapping population</h3>
<p id="p-43">Mature follicles were collected from the open-pollinated plant that was the subject of genome sequencing. Approximately 100 seeds from six follicles collected from four stems of this plant (1, 3, 1, and 1 follicle per stem) were germinated and grown at Oklahoma State University. Due to the pollination system of <i>Asclepias</i>, seeds in a fruit are likely to be fertilized by a single pollen donor (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Pollen%20compatibility%20in%20Asclepias%20syriaca&amp;author=Sparrow&amp;publication_year=1948" title="Pollen compatibility in Asclepias syriaca" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-111">Sparrow &amp; Pearson, 1948</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Reproductive%20biology%20of%20milkweeds%20(Asclepias):%20Recent%20advances&amp;author=Wyatt&amp;publication_year=1990" title="Reproductive biology of milkweeds (Asclepias): Recent advances" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-132">Wyatt &amp; Broyles, 1990</a>), meaning up to six paternal parents are represented among the 96 mapping offspring.</p>
<p id="p-44">Seeds were surface sterilized in 5% bleach and soaked for 24 h in distilled water. The testa was nicked opposite from the micropylar end and the seeds germinated on moist filter paper, in petri dishes, in the dark, at room temperature. Germination occurred within 4–7 days, and seedlings were planted into MetroMix 902 media in plug trays when radicles attained a length of two to three cm. Seedlings were again transplanted to three-inch deep pots following the expansion to two sets of true leaves. Seedlings were grown under high intensity fluorescent lights in a controlled environment chamber at 14 h daylength at approximately 27 °C. Plants were grown for approximately 90 days, harvested, and rinsed in distilled water, and frozen at −80 °C. DNA was extracted from roots, shoots, or a combination of roots and shoots using the FastDNA<sup>®</sup> kit (MP Biomedicals, Santa Ana, California) and Thermo Savant FastPrep<sup>®</sup> FP120 Cell Disrupter (Thermo Scientific, Waltham, MA, USA). DNA quantity and quality were visualized using agarose gel electrophoresis and quantified with a Qubit<sup>®</sup> fluorometer (Invitrogen, Carlsbad, CA, USA) and Quant-iT™ DNA-BR Assay Kit.</p>
<p id="p-45">A total of 96 genomic DNA samples were diluted as necessary with ultrapure water to obtain approximately three μg in 100 μl and sheared on a Bioruptor UCD-200 (Diagenode Inc., Denville, NJ, USA) at low power for 12 cycles of 30 s on/30 s off. Several samples required sonication for 5–10 additional cycles to achieve a high concentration of fragments at the target size of 300–400 bp. Illumina-compatible, dual-indexed libraries were produced with the TruSeq<sup>®</sup> HT kit (Illumina, San Diego, CA, USA), each with a unique barcode.</p>
<p id="p-46">Barcoded libraries were pooled by equal DNA mass in three groups of 32 samples. These were enriched for targeted SNP regions using RNA oligos and following MYcroarray MYbaits protocol v. 3.00. Enriched pools were then themselves evenly pooled and sequenced with 150 bp paired-end reads on an Illumina HiSeq 3000 at the CGRB, producing 120.3 Mbp of sequence data (NCBI short read archive: <a class="uri" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/SRX2163716">SRX2163716</a>–<a class="uri" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/SRX2163811">SRX2163811</a>).</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Linkage analyses</h3>
<p id="p-47">Reads from the 96 target-enriched offspring libraries were processed using Trimmomatic v. 0.33 to remove adapter sequences, bases on the ends of reads with a Phred quality score below three, and clipping once a sliding window of four bp fell below an average quality score of 17 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtu170" title="Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-17">Bolger, Lohse &amp; Usadel, 2014</a>). Processed reads for 90 samples (excluding six with low sequencing depth) were mapped onto the assembled scaffolds using bowtie2 with “sensitive” settings and a maximum fragment length of 600 bp (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fnmeth.1923" title="Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-68">Langmead &amp; Salzberg, 2012</a>). Reads from the 225 bp insert library of the sequenced individual were also mapped back onto assembled scaffolds using the same settings. Mappings for all individuals and the parent were combined using samtools v. 0.1.16 with the samtools “mpileup” command and flags “-D -S” to record the per-sample read depth and strand-specific bias. SNP positions were called using the bcftools “view” command with flags “-v -c -g” to output only potential variant sites with called genotypes (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Fbioinformatics%2Fbtp352" title="The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-72">Li et al., 2009</a>).</p>
<p id="p-48">Two subsets of SNPs were retained. The first was a subset of SNPs where the maternal parent was heterozygous and the paternal parents for all offspring were homozygous for the same allele. The file containing all variants was converted to a format suitable for the R package OneMap, using a custom perl script (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://github.com/jacobtennessen/MiSCVARS/blob/master/MakeOnemapFormatFromVcfNoParentsTwoGenos.pl" title="" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-117">Tennessen, 2015</a>), retaining only sites heterozygous in one parent, the maternal sequenced individual. In this filtering the minor genotype abundance (either heterozygote or homozygote) needed to be at least 24 across 90 samples, loci could have up to 30% missing individuals, and alternative genotypes within individuals were ignored if their Phred probability score was 15 or above (i.e., of the three possible genotypes AA, Aa, aa, one should be most probable with a low Phred score and the other two less probable with Phred scores above 15).</p>
<p id="p-49">The second subset retained SNPs from 22 full siblings (from the fruit producing the most offspring) for loci in which either the maternal or paternal parent, but not both, were heterozygous. Filtering in this set required a minor genotype abundance of at least five, loci could have up to four missing individuals, and genotypes with Phred probabilities of 20 or above were ignored (i.e., the final genotype calls are more certain because alternative genotypes are less likely).</p>
<p id="p-50">SNP sets were clustered into linkage groups in R v. 3.2.2 using the package OneMap v. 2.0-4 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fj.2007.0018-0661.02000.x" title="OneMap: software for genetic mapping in outcrossing species" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-81">Margarido, Souza &amp; Garcia, 2007</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="http://www.R-project.org/" title="R: a language and environment for statistical computing" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-98">R Core Team, 2014</a>). One SNP from each scaffold was selected from SNPs among the full set of individuals, and were grouped using a logarithm of odds (LOD) threshold of 8.4. This clustered SNP loci into 11 clear groups, referred to here as the core linkage groups.</p>
<p id="p-51">From the full-sibling set of SNPs, those held on the same scaffold and with identical genotypes across individuals (i.e., in perfect linkage) were grouped, and SNPs on different scaffolds in perfect linkage with no missing data were grouped. This was performed separately for loci where either the maternal or paternal parent was heterozygous. These loci were clustered into groups using LOD scores 6.1, 6.0, and 5.5. Each of these groupings produced hundreds of groups, but each contained about 22 groups that were substantially larger than the others.</p>
<p id="p-52">A custom R script was used to combine the linkage group identity of scaffolds in the core linkage groups with scaffolds and groups in the sibling sets (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.7267%2FN9M61HDR" title="Supplemental data to A draft genome and transcriptome of common milkweed (Asclepias syriaca) as resources for evolutionary, ecological, and molecular studies in milkweeds and Apocynaceae" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-127">Weitemier, 2017</a>). For example, scaffold A could be assigned to a linkage group if it was in perfect linkage in the sibling set with scaffold B, and scaffold B was also present in the core linkage groups. If multiple scaffolds were perfectly linked, but associated with different core linkage groups, no unknown scaffolds would be assigned unless the most common core linkage group was three times as common as the next core group.</p>
<p id="p-53">Linkage groupings in the sibling sets could be assigned to core linkage groups based on the membership of the scaffolds they contained. If the markers indicating that a sibling group should belong to a certain core linkage group were 10 times as common as markers supporting a second most common assignment, then the sibling group was assigned to the core group, and all unknown scaffolds it contained also assigned to that group. (For example, sibling group A contains 10 scaffolds known to be on core linkage group 1, one scaffold known to be on core linkage group 2, and one unknown scaffold; sibling group A is assigned to core linkage group 1 and the unknown scaffold is similarly assigned.)</p>
<p id="p-54">This process was performed iteratively, progressively assigning scaffolds to core linkage groups. It was performed first with the sibling set grouped with LOD 6.1, then the grouping with LOD 6.0, finally the grouping with LOD 5.5.</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Synteny within Gentianales</h3>
<p id="p-55">Scaffolds found within the core linkage groups were mapped to <i>Coffea</i> coding sequences (BLASTN, expect &lt;1, best hit chosen) and mapped to their location on <i>Coffea</i> pseudochromosomes. Six <i>Asclepias</i> linkage groups had a roughly one-to-one correspondence with a <i>Coffea</i> pseudochromosome (e.g., most of the scaffolds from that linkage group, and few from other linkage groups, mapped to the pseudochromosome). From these six linkage groups one marker was selected for every one Mbp segment of the <i>Coffea</i> chromosome. Recombination fractions were measured among these loci using OneMap (retaining “safe” markers with THRES=5) and converted to cM using the Kosambi mapping function.</p>
</section>
</section>
<section class="sec" id="results">
<h2 class="heading">Results</h2>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Sequencing and read processing</h3>
<p id="p-56">Paired-end sequencing produced 215.6 million pairs of reads representing 50.0 Gbp of sequence data, and mate-pair sequencing produced 52.8 million pairs of reads for 9.9 Gbp of sequence data. After read filtering and processing, 30.7 Gbp of paired-end sequence data remained along with 3.0 Gbp of mate-pair data. This represents total average sequence coverage of 80.4× on the 420 Mbp <i>Asclepias syriaca</i> genome (<a class="xref xref-table" href="#table-2" data-jats-ref-type="table" data-jats-rid="table-2">Table 2</a>).</p>
<p id="p-57">The distribution of 17 bp <i>k</i>-mers from the largest set of paired-end reads demonstrates a clear bi-modal distribution, with peaks at 43× and 84× depth (<a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-1" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-1">Fig. S1</a>), corresponding to the sequencing depth of heterozygous and homozygous portions of the genome, respectively. This <i>k</i>-mer distribution provides a genome size estimate of 406 Mbp, and a site heterozygosity rate estimate of 0.056.</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Sequence assembly and gene annotation</h3>
<p id="p-58">The assembly of <i>Asclepias syriaca</i> contains 54,266 scaffolds ≥1 kbp, with N50 = 3,415 bp, representing 37% of the estimated genome (156.6 Mbp of sequence plus 5.8 Mbp of gaps, <a class="xref xref-table" href="#table-3" data-jats-ref-type="table" data-jats-rid="table-3">Table 3</a>). When including scaffolds ≥200 bp the assembly sums to 229.7 Mbp, with N50 = 1,904 bp. The largest scaffold is 100 kbp, and 10% of the <i>Asclepias</i> genome, 42.82 Mbp, is held on 2,343 scaffolds ≥10 kbp. Prior to scaffolding, gap closing, contaminant removal, and transcript-assisted scaffolding, the initial assembly produced 2.8 million contigs, with 848,509 ≥ 200 bp and 38,615 ≥ 1 kbp. Initial contigs ≥1 kbp summed to 74.9 Mbp with N50 = 2,041 bp, and the largest contig was 16 kbp.</p>

</div>
</div>
</figure>
<p id="p-61">Within the 156.6 Mbp of scaffolds ≥1 kbp, 1.25 million putative ORFs were identified, along with 193 transfer RNA loci. Assembled repeat elements made up about 75.7 Mbp. A total of 14,474 protein-coding genes were identified based on transcript evidence, closely related proteins, and ab initio models. These are predicted to produce 15,628 unique mRNAs, and are made up of a total of 87,496 exons with an average length of 225.3 bp. The median length of predicted proteins is 303 amino acids (mean = 402), which is shorter than lengths predicted in <i>Calotropis</i> (median = 367, mean = 448), similar to those predicted in <i>Coffea</i> (median = 334, mean = 402), but longer than those predicted in <i>Catharanthus</i> (median = 251, mean = 340; <a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-2" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-2">Fig. 2</a>). Of the 14,474 predicted genes, 13,749 (95.0%) mapped to either <i>Coffea</i> or <i>Catharanthus</i> proteins, and 9,811 mapped to RefSeq proteins.</p>
<figure class="fig" itemprop="image" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject" id="fig-2"><div class="image-container"><a href="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-2-2x.jpg" title="View the full image" class="fresco" data-fresco-caption="Figure 2: Peptide length histograms of Asclepias, Calotropis, Coffea, and Catharanthus." data-fresco-group="figure" data-fresco-options="fit: 'width', ui: 'outside', thumbnails: false, loop: true, position: true, overflow: true, preload: false"><img class="graphic" src="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-2-1x.jpg" itemprop="contentUrl" sizes="(min-width: 1200px) 581px, (max-width: 1199px) and (min-width: 980px) 462px, (max-width: 979px) and (min-width: 768px) 347px, (max-width: 767px) calc(100vw - 50px)" srcset="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-2-2x.jpg 1200w, https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-2-1x.jpg 600w, https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-2-small.jpg 355w" data-image-id="fig-2" alt="Peptide length histograms of Asclepias, Calotropis, Coffea, and Catharanthus." data-full="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-2-full.png" data-thumb="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-2-thumb.jpg" data-original="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-2.png" data-image-type="figure" data-jats-mimetype="image" data-jats-mime-subtype="png" width="600" height="407"></a></div>

<div class="object-id article-component-doi">DOI: <a href="https://doi.org/10.7717/peerj.7649/fig-2" data-toggle="tooltip" title="Cite this object using this DOI">10.7717/peerj.7649/fig-2</a>
</div>
</div></figcaption></figure>
<p id="p-63">Assembly of the <i>Asclepias</i> transcriptome produced 32,728 best-scoring ORFs, ranging from 300 to 13,005 bp, with N50 = 1,422 bp, and summing to 37.4 Mbp. Of these, 31,654 (96.7%) mapped onto scaffolds ≥1 kbp. For <i>Calotropis</i>, 92,115 (88.4%) transcripts were mapped to <i>Asclepias</i> scaffolds, while 23,182 (90.6%) proteins from <i>Coffea</i> mapped to the assembly. BUSCO analysis of the genome assembly identified 895 of the 956 plant genes in its set (93.6%). Of these, 209 were identified as duplicates and another 77 genes were fragmented, meaning they were found in the genome assembly, but with a length outside two standard deviations of the mean BUSCO length for that gene. When applied to just the set of coding sequences BUSCO identified 742 complete genes (302 duplicated) and 84 fragmented genes, representing 86.4% of the conserved plant gene set. Apocynaceae transcriptomes were compared using the BUSCO set of 429 genes common to eukaryotes. The <i>Asclepias</i> transcriptome contained 365 of the genes (117 duplicated, 21 fragmented), representing 85.1%. Presence of these genes in other transcriptomes (<i>Catharanthus, Rauvolfia, Rhazya, Tabernaemontana, Calotropis</i>) ranged from 83.7% in <i>Calotropis</i> to 86.5% in <i>Tabernaemontana</i>, indicating that the <i>Asclepias</i> transcriptome assembly was of similar completeness to Apocynaceae transcriptomes publically available at the time of analysis. All Apocynaceae transcriptomes showed increased duplication of the 429 genes with approximately 2× the number of duplicates on average compared to the <i>Coffea</i>, <i>Catharanthus</i>, and <i>Vitis</i> genomes.</p>
<p id="p-64">Among 100,114 predicted genes from <i>Asclepias</i>, <i>Catharanthus</i>, <i>Coffea</i>, and <i>Vitis</i>, 69.9% were clustered into 13,906 orthogroups. <i>Asclepias</i> had the highest percentage of genes placed in orthogroups, 81.6%, but those genes only represent 9,837 orthogroups, the lowest of the four genomes. <i>Asclepias</i> shared the fewest orthogroups with other species (<a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-1" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-1">Table S1</a>).</p>
<p id="p-65">Comparison of all six Apocynaceae transcriptomes showed 5,195 gene families were common to all. The <i>Asclepias</i> transcriptome contained 5,762 gene families also present in the <i>Coffea</i> genome. There were 58 gene families with 1−3 gene copies in <i>Asclepias</i> that were not present in other Apocynaceae. Among Apocynaceae lineages, <i>Asclepias</i> was not unusual in its number of gene gains or losses based on the BadiRate analysis. <i>Asclepias</i> had close to the median number of gene gains among all lineages with 5,697 (median = 5,791.5), much less than the 15,831 gene gains inferred in the lineage with the highest number of gains, <i>Rauvolfia</i>. Similarly, the number of gene losses in <i>Asclepias</i> at 905 was just below the median number of losses (median = 1,136), and much less than the 7,619 losses inferred for <i>Catharanthus</i>. While <i>Asclepias</i> had one of the highest gene birth rates over time (0.01082 events per gene per million years; <a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-3" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-3">Fig. 3</a>), it was lower than that of close relative <i>Calotropis</i> (0.01463 events per gene per million years), and the rate inferred for the <i>Rauvolfia</i> plus <i>Catharanthus</i> plus <i>Tabernaemontana</i> lineage (0.14406 events per gene per million years) was an order of magnitude greater. <i>Asclepias</i> had close to the median value for gene death rate (0.00177 events per gene per million years). However, <i>Asclepias</i> had the second highest gene innovation rate (0.00069 events per gene per million years) compared to other lineages (<a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-3" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-3">Fig. 3</a>). As with gene birth rate, the gene innovation rate of the <i>Rauvolfia</i> plus <i>Catharanthus</i> plus <i>Tabernaemontana</i> lineage (0.00146 events per gene per million years) was an order of magnitude higher.</p>
<figure class="fig" itemprop="image" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject" id="fig-3"><div class="image-container"><a href="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-3-2x.jpg" title="View the full image" class="fresco" data-fresco-caption="Figure 3: Gene family evolution in Apocynaceae inferred from transcriptomes." data-fresco-group="figure" data-fresco-options="fit: 'width', ui: 'outside', thumbnails: false, loop: true, position: true, overflow: true, preload: false"><img class="graphic" src="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-3-1x.jpg" itemprop="contentUrl" sizes="(min-width: 1200px) 581px, (max-width: 1199px) and (min-width: 980px) 462px, (max-width: 979px) and (min-width: 768px) 347px, (max-width: 767px) calc(100vw - 50px)" srcset="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-3-2x.jpg 1200w, https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-3-1x.jpg 600w, https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-3-small.jpg 355w" data-image-id="fig-3" alt="Gene family evolution in Apocynaceae inferred from transcriptomes." data-full="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-3-full.png" data-thumb="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-3-thumb.jpg" data-original="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-3.png" data-image-type="figure" data-jats-mimetype="image" data-jats-mime-subtype="png" width="600" height="537"></a></div>

</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Linkage mapping and synteny within Gentianales</h3>
<p id="p-67">Following filtering, the set of all 96 offspring retained over 16,000 SNPs for which the maternal parent was heterozygous and all the paternal parents were homozygous for the same allele. These were located on 8,495 scaffolds, covering 43.5 Mbp. A total of 90 of 96 individuals were sequenced at adequate depth to inform linkage group analyses. At a logarithm of odds (LOD) score of 8.4, 7,809 scaffolds were clustered into 11 groups, the core linkage groups, representing 41.9 Mbp.</p>
<p id="p-68">Filtering for SNPs among just the largest group of full-siblings, in which one parent (but not both) was heterozygous, found 83,854 SNPs on 18,333 scaffolds. These SNPs were consolidated by perfect linkage and then clustered at LOD scores of 6.1, 6.0, and 5.5. Combining scaffolds from the core linkage groups with those clustered among the full-sibling group ultimately provided a combined linkage set, with linkage group assignments to 16,285 scaffolds, representing 75.0 Mbp.</p>
<p id="p-69">Mapping of scaffolds from just the core linkage groups to <i>Coffea</i> pseudochromosomes found several linkage group/pseudochromosome “best hit” pairs (e.g., most <i>Asclepias</i> scaffolds from a linkage group mapped to one pseudochromosome, while few scaffolds from other linkage groups mapped to that pseudochromosome). <i>Asclepias</i> linkage groups 2, 4, 6, 7, 8, and 9 mapped in this manner to <i>Coffea</i> pseudochromosomes 10, 8, 6, 11, 3, and 1, respectively (<a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-4" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-4">Figs. 4</a> and <a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-5" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-5">5</a>). From these six linkage groups, SNPs were chosen mapping to every one Mbp region (if available) of the corresponding <i>Coffea</i> pseudochromosome. Recombination distances were measured among these markers and their relative positions within <i>Asclepias</i> plotted against their position in <i>Coffea</i> (<a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-1" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-1">Figs. S2</a>–<a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-1" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-1">S7</a>). Monotonically increasing or decreasing series of points in these plots represent loci in <i>Asclepias</i> and <i>Coffea</i> that maintain their relative positions. Several such marker clusters are seen in these plots (<a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-1" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-1">Fig. S3</a>), though they tend to cover only short chromosomal regions and are often interrupted by markers from outside the cluster.</p>
<figure class="fig" itemprop="image" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject" id="fig-4"><div class="image-container"><a href="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-4-2x.jpg" title="View the full image" class="fresco" data-fresco-caption="Figure 4: Counts of Asclepias linkage group scaffolds mapping to Coffea pseudochromosomes." data-fresco-group="figure" data-fresco-options="fit: 'width', ui: 'outside', thumbnails: false, loop: true, position: true, overflow: true, preload: false"><img class="graphic" src="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-4-1x.jpg" itemprop="contentUrl" sizes="(min-width: 1200px) 581px, (max-width: 1199px) and (min-width: 980px) 462px, (max-width: 979px) and (min-width: 768px) 347px, (max-width: 767px) calc(100vw - 50px)" srcset="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-4-2x.jpg 1200w, https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-4-1x.jpg 600w, https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-4-small.jpg 355w" data-image-id="fig-4" alt="Counts of Asclepias linkage group scaffolds mapping to Coffea pseudochromosomes." data-full="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-4-full.png" data-thumb="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-4-thumb.jpg" data-original="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-4.png" data-image-type="figure" data-jats-mimetype="image" data-jats-mime-subtype="png" width="600" height="483"></a></div>

<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Progesterone 5β-reductase gene family</h3>
<p id="p-72">One region on linkage group 11 had 98.4% identity with peptide sequence from P5βR from <i>Asclepias curassavica</i> (<a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-1" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-1">Table S2</a>). This region was supported by <i>Asclepias syriaca</i> transcriptome evidence, as well as mapped <i>Calotropis</i> transcripts and <i>Coffea</i> proteins. Approximately 500 bp downstream from this gene, a second region was identified sharing 52% amino acid identity with the first region, for 70% of its length. The second region lacks transcript evidence from <i>Asclepias syriaca</i>, though portions of <i>Calotropis</i> transcripts and <i>Coffea</i> peptides map to it. Gene predictions from Augustus and SNAP include potential exons within the region, and the region includes P5βR conserved motifs I, II, and III, and portions of motifs IV, V, and VI described by <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1074%2Fjbc.M706185200" title="The crystal structure of progesterone 5β-reductase from Digitalis lanata defines a novel class of short chain dehydrogenases/reductases" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-120">Thorn et al. (2008)</a>. It is interpreted here as a pseudogene of P5βR, ΨP5βR (<a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-1" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-1">Table S2</a>).</p>
<p id="p-73">Paralogs of P5βR have been described in other angiosperms including <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>Populus</i>, <i>Vitis</i>, and <i>Digitalis</i>, and the P5βR2 paralog occurs on a chromosome separate from that of P5βR1 in <i>Arabidopsis</i> and <i>Populus</i> (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fj.1469-8137.2009.03080.x" title="Digitalis purpurea P5βR2, encoding steroid 5β-reductase, is a novel defense-related gene involved in cardenolide biosynthesis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-93">Pérez-Bermúdez et al., 2010</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.phytochem.2010.06.004" title="Highly conserved progesterone 5β-reductase genes (P5βR) from 5β-cardenolide-free and 5β-cardenolide-producing angiosperms" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-15">Bauer et al., 2010</a>). Due to frame shifts and ambiguous exon boundaries in ΨP5βR, it is difficult to assess the correct peptide sequence it initially encoded, and therefore difficult to fully align with <i>Digitalis</i> P5βR1 and P5βR2 sequences. However, a few motifs, particularly a triple tryptophan at the N-terminal end of the sequence, suggest its origin from P5βR1, a conclusion supported by its position adjacent to the coding P5βR in <i>Asclepias</i>.</p>
<p id="p-74">A third region on an unlinked scaffold exhibited moderate (37%) identity with the peptide sequence from linkage group 11 (<a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-1" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-1">Table S2</a>). This region includes an intact reading frame and is matched by transcripts from <i>Calotropis</i>, though a lack of <i>Asclepias</i> transcripts matching this region indicates that it may not be regularly expressed within leaves or buds. A peptide alignment was made for this sequence, the known coding P5βR in <i>Asclepias</i>, and P5βR sequences from <i>Asclepias curassavica</i>, <i>Calotropis procera</i>, <i>Calotropis gigantea</i>, <i>Rhazya</i>, <i>Digitalis</i>, <i>Catharanthus</i>, and <i>Picea</i> to infer the phylogeny of this locus. The optimal model of sequence evolution selected by AIC was the LG+G+I model of peptide substitution, rate variation among sites, and proportion of invariable sites (BIC selected the LG+G model, but tree topologies were identical and are not shown). A maximum-likelihood estimate of the P5βR gene tree grouped the unlinked <i>Asclepias</i> sequence with a paralog from <i>Rhazya</i> (originating on supercontig 3 from <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fsrep33782" title="The nuclear genome of Rhazya stricta and the evolution of alkaloid diversity in a medically relevant clade of Apocynaceae" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-104">Sabir et al., 2016</a>) and <i>Catharanthus</i> paralog P5βR6 (<a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-6" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-6">Fig. 6</a>). Together these are sister to all other P5βR sequences analyzed, except <i>Picea</i>, which was used to root the gene tree. The P5βR sequence from linkage group 11 is strongly supported as the most closely related sequence to the one from <i>Asclepias curassavica</i>, within a clade including P5βR1 sequences from <i>Digitalis</i> and <i>Catharanthus</i>.</p>
<figure class="fig" itemprop="image" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="https://schema.org/ImageObject" id="fig-6"><div class="image-container"><a href="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-6-2x.jpg" title="View the full image" class="fresco" data-fresco-caption="Figure 6: Maximum likelihood phylogeny of progesterone 5β-reductase paralogs." data-fresco-group="figure" data-fresco-options="fit: 'width', ui: 'outside', thumbnails: false, loop: true, position: true, overflow: true, preload: false"><img class="graphic" src="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-6-1x.jpg" itemprop="contentUrl" sizes="(min-width: 1200px) 581px, (max-width: 1199px) and (min-width: 980px) 462px, (max-width: 979px) and (min-width: 768px) 347px, (max-width: 767px) calc(100vw - 50px)" srcset="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-6-2x.jpg 1200w, https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-6-1x.jpg 600w, https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-6-small.jpg 355w" data-image-id="fig-6" alt="Maximum likelihood phylogeny of progesterone 5β-reductase paralogs." data-full="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-6-full.png" data-thumb="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-6-thumb.jpg" data-original="https://dfzljdn9uc3pi.cloudfront.net/2019/7649/1/fig-6.png" data-image-type="figure" data-jats-mimetype="image" data-jats-mime-subtype="png" width="600" height="535"></a></div>
<figcaption itemprop="description">
<h4 class="heading">
<span class="caption-label">Figure 6: </span>Maximum likelihood phylogeny of progesterone 5β-reductase paralogs.</h4>
<span class="p"><i>Asclepias syriaca</i> labels indicate the linkage group from which that sequence originates. <i>Catharanthus</i> and <i>Digitalis</i> labels indicate numbered paralogs isolated from that species. <i>Rhazya</i> labels indicate the originating supercontig from <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fsrep33782" title="The nuclear genome of Rhazya stricta and the evolution of alkaloid diversity in a medically relevant clade of Apocynaceae" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-104">Sabir et al. (2016)</a> with two paralogs coming from supercontig 17. <i>Calotropis procera</i> labels indicate the originating transcript from <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0119328" title="Cysteine protease profiles of the medicinal plant Calotropis procera R.
