</div></figcaption></figure>
<p id="p-76">Analysis of the P5βR gene family across Apocynaceae showed that this gene family is largest in <i>Rauvolfia</i>, <i>Catharanthus</i>, and <i>Tabernaemontana</i>, with most of the expansion occurring in the common ancestor of these three (<a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-3" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-3">Fig. 3</a>). However, this interpretation may change as more Apocynaceae genomes and transcriptomes become available.</p>
</section>
</section>
<section class="sec" id="discussion">
<h2 class="heading">Discussion</h2>
<p id="p-77">The <i>Asclepias syriaca</i> nuclear genome assembly presented here represents a large fraction of the protein-coding gene space, despite very high levels of heterozygosity and sequence data restricted to Illumina short reads. Gene space coverage is supported by high proportions of BUSCO plant core genes found within the assembly (93.6%) as well as assembled transcripts mapping to the assembly (96.7%). A substantial portion of genes from related plant species mapped to the assembly as well, including 88.4% of transcripts from <i>Calotropis</i> and 90.6% of amino acid sequences from <i>Coffea</i>.</p>
<p id="p-78">Overall, the <i>Asclepias</i> assembly is fragmented when compared to other plant genomes assembled using either long reads or deep sequencing of known contiguous fragments (e.g., BACs or fosmids), and inclusion of these technologies in future assembly efforts should result in a more complete and contiguous assembly. Assembly was also hindered by poor quality mate-pair libraries containing low proportions of properly paired fragments (<a class="xref xref-table" href="#table-2" data-jats-ref-type="table" data-jats-rid="table-2">Table 2</a>). However, assembly results are typical for a sequencing project relying entirely on short reads, especially for organisms with high levels of heterozygosity. For example, the <i>Asclepias</i> N50 value of 3.4 kbp compares favorably to the assembly of the rubber tree, <i>Hevea brasiliensis</i>, genome (N50 = 2,972 bp; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-75" title="Draft genome sequence of the rubber tree Hevea brasiliensis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-99">Rahman et al., 2013</a>), though it is not as contiguous as the dwarf birch, <i>Betula nana</i>, genome (N50 = 18.6 kbp; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fmec.12131" title="Genome sequence of dwarf birch (Betula nana) and cross-species RAD markers" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-125">Wang et al., 2012</a>), which incorporated several mate pair libraries. The assembly of the olive tree, <i>Olea europaea</i>, genome was also very similar to <i>Asclepias</i>, with N50 = 3.8 kbp prior to the inclusion of fosmid libraries (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1186%2Fs13742-016-0134-5" title="Genome sequence of the olive tree, Olea europaea" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-23">Cruz et al., 2016</a>). However, paired-end and mate-pair data for <i>Calotropis gigantea</i> provide an example of a less fragmented assembly from similar data (N50 = 805 kbp, <a class="xref xref-table" href="#table-1" data-jats-ref-type="table" data-jats-rid="table-1">Table 1</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1534%2Fg3.117.300331" title="Genome assembly and annotation of the medicinal plant Calotropis gigantea, a producer of anticancer and antimalarial cardenolides" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-48">Hoopes et al., 2018</a>). The effect of high heterozygosity is clearly seen in the comparison of <i>Asclepias</i> and <i>Catharanthus</i> assemblies (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Ftpj.12827" title="Genome-guided investigation of plant natural product biosynthesis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-58">Kellner et al., 2015</a>). While sequence data and genome assembly methods are similar between the two, <i>Asclepias</i> has an estimated heterozygosity rate of &gt;1 SNP per 20 bp, whereas the heterozygosity rate in the inbred <i>Catharanthus</i> cultivar is estimated at &lt;1 SNP per 1,000 bp. This resulted in a N50 of 27.3 kbp assembled from only a single <i>Catharanthus</i> Illumina library (<a class="xref xref-table" href="#table-1" data-jats-ref-type="table" data-jats-rid="table-1">Table 1</a>).</p>
<p id="p-79">Functional annotations were applied to a high proportion (95.0%) of the 14,474 called genes, which were mapped to proteins from <i>Catharanthus roseus</i> and/or to <i>Coffea canephora</i>. The number of called genes is well below the typical value for plant genomes: the genome of <i>Calotropis gigantea</i>, the closest relative with an assembled genome, contains 19,536 gene loci (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1534%2Fg3.117.300331" title="Genome assembly and annotation of the medicinal plant Calotropis gigantea, a producer of anticancer and antimalarial cardenolides" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-48">Hoopes et al., 2018</a>). The genomes of <i>Rhazya</i> and <i>Catharanthus</i> contain 21,164 and 33,829 called genes, respectively (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Ftpj.12827" title="Genome-guided investigation of plant natural product biosynthesis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-58">Kellner et al., 2015</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fsrep33782" title="The nuclear genome of Rhazya stricta and the evolution of alkaloid diversity in a medically relevant clade of Apocynaceae" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-104">Sabir et al., 2016</a>). The genome of <i>Coffea</i> contains 25,574 protein-coding genes, and the genome of tomato, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, from the sister order, Solanales, contains 36,148 (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fnature11119" title="The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-119">The Tomato Genome Consortium, 2012</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1126%2Fscience.1255274" title="The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-24">Denoeud et al., 2014</a>).</p>
<p id="p-80">It is likely that the gene count in <i>Catharanthus</i> is an overestimate, a possibility in fragmented genome assemblies (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pcbi.1003998" title="Extensive error in the number of genes inferred from draft genome assemblies" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-25">Denton et al., 2014</a>), as indicated by the excess of short predicted proteins relative to <i>Coffea</i> and <i>Calotropis</i> (<a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-2" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-2">Fig. 2</a>). By contrast, the 14,474 called genes in <i>Asclepias</i> is likely an underestimate of the true number. While the size distribution of predicted <i>Asclepias</i> proteins is quite similar to that of <i>Coffea</i>, <i>Asclepias</i> contains fewer proteins of all sizes, and the dramatic reduction of orthogroups found in <i>Asclepias</i> relative to other species argues for deficiency in gene calling. While it’s possible that similar genes were mistakenly collapsed into a single contig during the assembly stage meant to collapse alleles at a single locus, this should only occur with genes isolated on small contigs and should not affect the number of orthogroups identified. Nevertheless, the high proportion of matches between the <i>Asclepias</i> genome assembly, <i>Asclepias</i> transcripts, and gene sets from related organisms, indicates that the assembly likely does contain sequence information for nearly the full complement of genes, but that some of these have not been recognized by gene calling algorithms due to the fragmented nature of the assembly.</p>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Synteny within Gentianales</h3>
<p id="p-81">A total of 11 core linkage groups were produced from the set of SNPs originating from the maternal parent, matching the expected number from a single parent with <i>x</i> = <i>n</i> = 11 chromosomes. Using full-siblings, a set of SNPs originating from either the maternal or paternal parent clustered into hundreds of groups. A total of 22 of these were substantially larger than the others, matching the expectation of 22 linkage groups originating from two <i>x</i> = 11 parents.</p>
<p id="p-82">Six of the 11 core linkage groups in <i>Asclepias</i> show high synteny at a chromosomal scale with the pseudochromosomes of <i>Coffea</i> (<a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-4" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-4">Figs. 4</a> and <a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-5" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-5">5</a>). This suggests that these chromosomes have remained largely stable and retained the same gene content for over 95 Myr, throughout the evolution of the Gentianales (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0126690" title="A revised time tree of the Asterids: establishing a temporal framework for evolutionary studies of the coffee family (Rubiaceae)" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-129">Wikström et al., 2015</a>). These stable chromosomes may have remained largely intact for a much longer period as well. The stable <i>Coffea</i> pseudochromosomes (1, 3, 6, 8, 10, and 11) retain largely the same content as inferred for ancestral core eudicot chromosomes, exhibiting little fractionation, even after an inferred genome triplication at the base of the eudicots, 117–125 Myr ago (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1098%2Frstb.2013.0355" title="Polyploidy-associated genome modifications during land plant evolution" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-54">Jiao &amp; Paterson, 2014</a>; see Figure 1B in <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1126%2Fscience.1255274" title="The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-24">Denoeud et al. (2014)</a>).</p>
<p id="p-83">Despite the conservation of gene content, gene order within stable chromosomes may be more labile. Plots of recombination distance among markers in <i>Asclepias</i> against physical distance in <i>Coffea</i> show several sets of markers in <i>Coffea</i> that retain their relative order in <i>Asclepias</i>, but are frequently interrupted by loci found elsewhere on the same <i>Coffea</i> pseudochromosome. For example, within <i>Asclepias</i> linkage group 2 there is a set of markers that retain their same relative ordering from positions three million to eight million on <i>Coffea</i> pseudochromosome 10 (<a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-1" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-1">Fig. S2</a>). However, these markers in <i>Asclepias</i> are interrupted by markers mapping to positions closer to the origin on the same <i>Coffea</i> pseudochromosome as well as a marker mapping to the far end. The most conserved synteny is between <i>Asclepias</i> linkage group 8 and <i>Coffea</i> pseudochromosome 3, which show complete synteny except for an apparent transposition of markers at positions two million and seven million on <i>Coffea</i> pseudochromosome 3 (<a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-1" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-1">Fig. S3</a>).</p>
<p id="p-84">Contrasting the stability in gene content of six <i>Coffea</i> pseudochromosomes, pseudochromosome 2 is inferred to contain portions of at least five ancestral core eudicot chromosomes. This suggests significant fractionation in this chromosome since the eudicot triplication event (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1126%2Fscience.1255274" title="The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-24">Denoeud et al., 2014</a>). Even between <i>Coffea</i> and <i>Asclepias</i>, pseudochromosome 2 maps to portions of several <i>Asclepias</i> linkage groups (<a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-4" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-4">Figs. 4</a> and <a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-5" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-5">5</a>). Therefore, the fractionation within this chromosome appears to have either occurred only within the branch leading from the Gentianales ancestor to <i>Coffea</i>, or occurred earlier and then continued along the branch leading to <i>Asclepias</i>. If the latter is true, then a higher frequency of rearrangement may be a characteristic of this chromosome within the Gentianales, relative to other chromosomes. Analyses of chromosomal rearrangements in <i>Rhazya</i> (Figure 1 in <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fsrep33782" title="The nuclear genome of Rhazya stricta and the evolution of alkaloid diversity in a medically relevant clade of Apocynaceae" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-104">Sabir et al. (2016)</a>) support this view, suggesting several rearrangements between the core eudicot triplication event and the Gentianales ancestor, and continued rearrangement between that ancestor and <i>Rhazya</i>. However, mapped genomic resources within other Asterids outside of Gentianales are scarce, and are only found in taxa that have undergone additional genome duplication events since the eudicot triplication (e.g., <i>Solanum</i>, <i>Daucus</i>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fnature11119" title="The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-119">The Tomato Genome Consortium, 2012</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fng.3565" title="A high-quality carrot genome assembly provides new insights into carotenoid accumulation and asterid genome evolution" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-52">Iorizzo et al., 2016</a>), complicating synteny assessments that might resolve the timing of fractionation of this chromosome.</p>
<p id="p-85">The production of physical maps of both <i>Asclepias</i> and <i>Coffea</i> chromosomes will help resolve how frequently synteny has been disturbed between the two taxa. The ordered scaffold maps presented here (<a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-1" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-1">Figs. S2</a>–<a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-1" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-1">S7</a>) contain only a few dozen markers, and trends apparent now could be altered on maps with much greater resolution. The <i>Coffea</i> pseudochromosomes, meanwhile, are still ultimately ordered by recombination frequency, and about half of the scaffolds are placed with unknown orientation (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1126%2Fscience.1255274" title="The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-24">Denoeud et al., 2014</a>), which could manifest here as apparent transpositions among adjacent markers.</p>
</section>
<section class="sec">
<h3 class="heading">Progesterone 5β-reductase gene family</h3>
<p id="p-86">The name <i>Asclepias</i> comes from the Greek god of medicine, Asclepius, whose name was applied to this genus for its potent secondary compounds. The cardenolides of <i>Asclepias</i> belong to a class of steroidal compounds, cardiac glycosides, used to treat cardiac insufficiency. While the genetic pathway that produces β-cardenolides (the form of cardenolide that includes the medicinal compound digitoxin) is largely unknown, one of the early steps involves the conversion of progesterone to 5β-pregnane-3,20-dione (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2F0014-5793%2890%2980415-F" title="A stereospecific enzyme of the putative biosynthetic pathway of cardenolides" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-38">Gärtner, Wendroth &amp; Seitz, 1990</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fj.1432-1033.1994.1125b.x" title="Purification, characterization and partial peptide microsequencing of progesterone 5β-reductase from shoot cultures of Digitalis purpurea" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-37">Gärtner, Keilholz &amp; Seitz, 1994</a>), catalyzed by the enzyme P5βR. Orthologs of P5βR occur broadly across seed plants, even in taxa that do not produce β-cardenolides, including <i>Asclepias</i>, which only produces α-cardenolides (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.phytochem.2010.06.004" title="Highly conserved progesterone 5β-reductase genes (P5βR) from 5β-cardenolide-free and 5β-cardenolide-producing angiosperms" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-15">Bauer et al., 2010</a>). The P5βR1 locus has been characterized in <i>Asclepias curassavica</i>, but information about its genomic context has remained unknown.</p>
<p id="p-87">A coding P5βR ortholog was located in <i>Asclepias syriaca</i> on linkage group 11, sharing 98.4% amino acid identity with P5βR from <i>Asclepias curassavica</i>. This gene is supported by transcripts from <i>Asclepias</i>, as well as mapped transcripts from <i>Calotropis</i> and proteins from <i>Coffea</i>. The presence of a novel P5βR pseudogene was also identified closely downstream from the expressed gene (<a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-1" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-1">Table S2</a>). Sharing high identity with the expressed P5βR, including several conserved motifs, it clearly originated from a P5βR duplication at some point. However, it is assumed to be non-functional due to its degraded exons interrupted by multiple stop codons and lack of expression evidence from the transcriptome.</p>
<p id="p-88">A third region in <i>Asclepias</i>, on an unlinked scaffold, was matched by multiple P5βR sequences from <i>Catharanthus</i> (<a class="xref xref-supplementary-material" href="#supp-1" data-jats-ref-type="supplementary-material" data-jats-rid="supp-1">Table S2</a>). This region is made up of a single ORF that shares only moderate identity with the <i>Asclepias</i> coding P5βR, and is not supported by <i>Asclepias</i> transcript evidence. In a P5βR phylogeny, the unlinked <i>Asclepias</i> region is sister to <i>Catharanthus</i> P5βR6 and a copy from <i>Rhazya</i> (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Ftpj.12827" title="Genome-guided investigation of plant natural product biosynthesis" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-58">Kellner et al., 2015</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fsrep33782" title="The nuclear genome of Rhazya stricta and the evolution of alkaloid diversity in a medically relevant clade of Apocynaceae" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-104">Sabir et al., 2016</a>). These sequences together are sister to all other P5βR sequences analyzed except <i>Picea</i>, which was used for rooting (<a class="xref xref-fig" href="#fig-6" data-jats-ref-type="fig" data-jats-rid="fig-6">Fig. 6</a>).</p>
<p id="p-89">While at least two P5βR paralogs have been identified in a wide range of plants, and <i>Rhazya</i>, <i>Rauvolfia</i>, <i>Catharanthus</i>, and <i>Tabernaemontana</i> exhibit expression evidence of multiple paralogs, <i>Asclepias</i> is reduced for this group of genes. <i>Rauvolfia</i> and <i>Tabernaemontana</i> are known to produce cardenolides, but <i>Catharanthus</i> and <i>Rhazya</i> do not (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fj.1469-8137.2011.04049.x" title="Toxic cardenolides: chemical ecology and coevolution of specialized plant-herbivore interactions" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-5">Agrawal et al., 2012</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Preliminary%20phytochemical%20analysis%20of%20Tabernaemontana%20alternifolia&amp;author=Sivagnanam&amp;publication_year=2014" title="Preliminary phytochemical analysis of Tabernaemontana alternifolia" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-108">Sivagnanam &amp; Kumar, 2014</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Antisickling%20and%20toxicological%20evaluation%20of%20the%20leaves%20of%20Rauwolfia%20vomitoria%20Afzel%20(Apocynaceae)&amp;author=Abere&amp;publication_year=2014" title="Antisickling and toxicological evaluation of the leaves of Rauwolfia vomitoria Afzel (Apocynaceae)" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-1">Abere et al., 2014</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1534%2Fg3.117.300331" title="Genome assembly and annotation of the medicinal plant Calotropis gigantea, a producer of anticancer and antimalarial cardenolides" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-48">Hoopes et al., 2018</a>). <i>Calotropis</i> is known to produce β-cardenolides (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.phytochem.2010.06.004" title="Highly conserved progesterone 5β-reductase genes (P5βR) from 5β-cardenolide-free and 5β-cardenolide-producing angiosperms" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-15">Bauer et al., 2010</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1038%2Fsrep34464" title="Transcriptome and metabolite analysis reveal candidate genes of the cardiac glycoside biosynthetic pathway from Calotropis procera" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-90">Pandey et al., 2016</a>), and contains two P5βR paralogs (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1534%2Fg3.117.300331" title="Genome assembly and annotation of the medicinal plant Calotropis gigantea, a producer of anticancer and antimalarial cardenolides" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-48">Hoopes et al., 2018</a>). It is possible that the fragmented nature of the current assembly precludes identification of all existing P5βR paralogs in <i>Asclepias syriaca</i>, however, both genome assembly and transcript evidence point toward one functional P5βR locus. While multiple genes are involved in the production of β-cardenolides, it may be that the reduction in the P5βR family is responsible for the lack of these compounds in <i>Asclepias</i>, which only contains α-cardenolides.</p>
</section>
</section>
<section class="sec" id="conclusions">
<h2 class="heading">Conclusions</h2>
<p id="p-90">We present a draft genome assembly with linkage information of <i>Asclepias syriaca</i>, assigning nearly half of scaffolds to linkage groups. While the assembly remains fragmented, multiple lines of evidence indicate that nearly all of the gene space of <i>Asclepias</i> is represented within the assembly.</p>
<p id="p-91">Linkage information allowed assessment of synteny across the order Gentianales. Six of 11 chromosomes retain similar gene content across the order, and these chromosomes have likely remained stable since the divergence of eudicots. One chromosome has either experienced dramatic fractionation since the divergence of Rubiaceae from other Gentianales, or experienced earlier fractionation that continued within Gentianales.</p>
<p id="p-92"><i>Asclepias syriaca</i> and its relatives are important systems for a wide range of evolutionary and ecological studies, and are an important component of many ecosystems, serving as prolific nectar producers and as hosts to a range of specially adapted species. The availability of the <i>Asclepias</i> genome, coupled with genomic data from symbiotic organisms, particularly insects, promises to inform important mechanisms of co-evolution (<a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1073%2Fpnas.0802368105" title="Phylogenetic escalation and decline of plant defense strategies" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-4">Agrawal &amp; Fishbein, 2008</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.cell.2011.09.052" title="The Monarch butterfly genome yields insights into long-distance migration" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-136">Zhan et al., 2011</a>; <a class="xref xref-bibr" href="https://doi.org/10.1073%2Fpnas.1503926112" title="The butterfly plant arms-race escalated by gene and genome duplications" data-jats-ref-type="bibr" data-jats-rid="ref-27">Edger et al., 2015</a>). We expect that the data presented here will advance these studies and aid the discovery of novel insights into the origin and evolution of a charismatic family, the production of important secondary compounds, and the ecological and evolutionary relationships between milkweeds and their communities.</p>
</section>

<script>
    (function(){
        $('#download-subscribe-submit').click(function(){

            var button = $(this);
            var url = button.data('url');
            if(button.attr('disabled')) return;

            $.get(url, function(response){

                if(!response.token){
                    errorContainer.html('Server error, you have not been subscribed').show();
                    button.html('Subscribe').removeAttr('disabled');
                    return;
                }

                var errorContainer = $('#download-subscribe-error-container');
                errorContainer.html('').hide();
                button.html('<i class="icon-spin icon-spinner"></i>').attr('disabled', true);

                var signedIn = button.data('signed-in');
                var sectionName = button.data('section-name');
                var data = {
                    _token: response.token
                };

                if(!signedIn) {
                    var email = $('#download-subscribe-email').val();
                    data.email = email;
                    data.freq = $('download-subscribe-freq').val();
                }

                $.ajax({
                    url: url,
                    method: 'POST',
                    data: data
                }).success(function(response){
                    button.hide();
                    $('.js-close-download-modal').trigger('click');

                    PeerJ.Tools.ToastNotifications.add({
                        type: 'success',
                        title: 'Subscribed',
                        text: sectionName ? 'You subscribed to ' + sectionName : 'You subscribed to this article\'s subjects'
                    });

                }).error(function(response){
                    if(response.responseJSON && response.responseJSON.errors){
                        errorContainer.html(response.responseJSON.errors[0]).show();
                    }
                }).complete(function(){
                    button.html('Subscribe').removeAttr('disabled');
                });

            });
        });

    }());
</script>
        </div>

                            <label><strong>Type of problem</strong></label>
                            <p>
                            <select id="moderation_flag_category" name="moderation_flag[category]" class="span4"><option value="typo">Typo</option><option value="metadata">Missing or incorrect metadata</option><option value="quality">Quality: PDF, figure, table, or data quality</option><option value="download">Download issues</option><option value="abuse">Abusive behavior</option><option value="misconduct">Research misconduct</option><option value="other">Other issue not listed above</option></select>
                            
                            </p>
                         </div>
                        <div>
                            <label><strong>Details</strong> <i class="icon-large icon-question-sign" title="Please be as detailed as possible within the 500 character limit. Any details you provide will not be shown publicly." data-toggle="tooltip"></i></label>
                            <div>
                                <textarea id="moderation_flag_detail" name="moderation_flag[detail]" required="required" maxlength="500" class="span4" placeholder="Enter any details about this issue. Kept confidential with PeerJ staff." rows="5" data-counter-target="#flag-counter"></textarea>
                                
                                <div style="margin:10px 0 0 0; color:#777777; float: left; display: block"><span id="flag-counter" class="label">500</span> characters remaining</div>
                            </div>
                         </div>
                        
                                    </div>

          </div>
        </form>
        <div id="flag-modal-footer" class="modal-footer">
        <button class="btn" data-dismiss="modal" aria-hidden="true">Cancel</button>
        <input type="submit" class="btn btn-success save-flag-btn" value="Send report">
        </div>
</div>

                <script>
            (function(){

                function initTweets(){
                    //Get count
                    var tweetCountUrl = '/articles/7649/tweet-count/';
                    $.get(tweetCountUrl, function(response){
                        if(response.count){
                            $('#tweet-count-container').html('View ' + response.count + ' tweet' + (response.count > 1 ? 's' : ''));
                            $('#btn-view-tweets').fadeIn(200);
                        }
                    });

                    //Get first page
                    $('.tweet-items').html('Loading...');
                    var tweetsPageUrl = '/articles/7649/tweets/';
                    $.get(tweetsPageUrl, function(response){
                        if(response.html){
                            $('.tweet-items').html(response.html);
                            $("html, body").animate({scrollTop: 0}, 500);
                        }
                    });
                }

                setTimeout(initTweets, 2500);

            })();
        </script>

                
        <script>
            $(function () {
                // syntax highlighting for code blocks
                $("pre > code").each(function() {
                    var node = $(this);

                    var language;

                    // JATS >=1.1
                    language = node.data('jats-language');

                    if (!language) {
                        // JATS <1.1
                        language = node.data('jats-preformat-type');

                        // ignore default 'code' type
                        if (language === 'code') {
                            language = null;
                        }
                    }

                    if (language) {
                        node.addClass('language-' + language);
                    }

                    hljs.highlightBlock(this);
                });
            });
        </script>
    <!--<![endif]-->
